ii
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Optical surface navigation on planetary bodies is the concept of estimating the pose of a
camera based on images. This concept was proven to be reliable to reach Technology
Readiness Level (TRL) of 9, which was flight proven by Mars 2020. This system, Lander
Vision System (LVS), is based on Terrain­Relative Navigation (TRN), where landmark­
s/features detected on an onboard camera are matched to features in a reference image
with corresponding Digital Elevation Model (DEM). This mission has made the reference
maps freely available in addition to the descent images, which has been the starting point
of this project. This project will focus on making an algorithm which is able to estimate
the pose of a camera based on feature matching. To achieve this, then the Mars 2020
data are used to develop the initial algorithm, where it can be tested in the Optical Nav­
igation Lab (ONL), where the maps have to be generated as well. The scope of this
project involves feature matching, pose estimation, Digital Terrain Model (DTM) and or­
thophoto generation from stereo images, and finally combining the pose estimation with
the generated DTM and orthophoto. The project is based on implementation in MATLAB,
where built­in function are used for feature matching and pose estimation by solving the
Perspective­n­Point (PnP) problem.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
iii

Acknowledgements
Mathias Benn, Associate Professor, National Space Institute, Denmark
I would like to express my deepest appreciation to Mathias Benn, who made it possible
to do a project in this field and offered guidance and motivation though the entire project
period to overcome obstacles on the way. Furthermore, this project has allowed me to go
in depth of optical navigation and dig into different fields which I appreciate.
I would likewise also thank David Mayer from United States Geological Survey (USGS)
(Astrogeology Science Center) and Andrew Johnson from Jet Propulsion Laboratory (JPL),
who help to clarify information from the Mars 2020 mission.
And probably the biggest thank you to my family for the support throughout the past
months, which made it possible to focus on this project. And thank you for all the support
throughout the years at DTU, that has meant so much.

Glossary
Glossary
CAHVOR Camera model typically used by NASA/JPL for cameras, which contains the
radial distortion coefficients in addition to CAHVOR. xi, 18, 19
CAHVOR Camera model typically used by NASA/JPL for cameras, which contains the
intrinsics and the extrinsic values in vectors. xi, 18, 61
CAHVORE Camera model typically used by NASA/JPL for cameras, where this specific
camera model is expaned with an additional term, E, which models the fish eye lens
effect, or entrance pupil, based on CAHVOR. 3, 19, 62, 110, 111, 127
ECM A dataproduct which for the case of Mars 2020, is the data received from the rover,
and therefore the raw measurements. 4
Mars 2020 Mars 2020 is the latest rover also known as Perseverance, which landed in
Jezero Crater. iii–ix, xi, 1–11, 18, 19, 30–32, 35–38, 40, 42–44, 60–62, 65, 66,
68–78, 80, 82, 85, 106–111, 127, 128, 130–138
MSL The Mars Science Laboratory (MSL) rover, also known as Curiosity, which was the
predecessor to . 3
Sol A day on Mars. 62
SWAP Computer memory based on the computer main drive instead of Random Access
Memory (RAM). 60

Chapter 1. Introduction and problem formulation
1
Introduction and problem formulation
Ever since the dawn of mankind, space has always been something been awe­inspiring
for humans. This has lead to great achievements in technology which is sometimes taken
for granted such as the everyday Global Navigation Satellite System (GNSS). However,
as the interest for exploring new planetary surfaces and moons have never been higher,
one challenge still persist: How to know where a landing spacecraft is heading. SpaceX
rockets currently use GNSS based positioning to land its first stage rockets, however,
this can not be used on any near term missions to other planetary bodies, as GNSS
requires a supporting infrastructure. However, looking back in time before the era of
GNSS, then other technologies were in use that used optical or radar data to get a position
of a cruise missile.[1] This was the start of relative optical navigation, where a visual input
from cameras were used to get a position relative to certain maps. However, as the
Global Positioning System (GPS) constellation was deployed, then this technology had
a successor able to work without visual inputs. However, having no GNSS is exactly the
case when landing on the Moon, Mars etc. where the only known inputs are from previous
mapping missions and onboard sensors. Landing at the these surfaces typical comes at
a risk, as hazardous terrain can lead to a mission ending prior to the beginning. And
having a commercial interest, which has never been higher, for missions to the surface
of the Moon and Mars, requires technologies which can make this landing as safe as
possible. The technology can be divided into two main parts; the hardware responsible
to land the vehicle safely on the surface, and software in order to estimate the current
position and orientation in order to avoid the most hazardous places. This project has
solely been focused on the software part, where it has been the main motivation for this
thesis, as the basic technology of relative navigation on other planetary surfaces than
Earth is needed. The current state of the art, which has been proven on a real mission, is
the LVS onboard Mars 2020 developed by JPL, which achieved far better accuracy than
anticipated, as the final accuracy of the landing target was within 5 m, which was almost
an order of magnitude better compared to the required 40 m. The LVS system, developed
by JPL, has been in the works since 2004, which shows how much effort that has been
done in order to have this achievement. Even though, that the method behind LVS has
achieved such great results, then slightly different ways is interesting to study as well,
in order to see what possibilities exist. Many different ways are currently being studied,
and some are planned to be used for robotic lunar landers at the south pole of the Moon,
where shadowing craters makes the process more challenging. [1]
This project will be about using methods from the field of computer vision, to estimate
the pose of a camera above a mapped terrain. Due to experienced difficulties of using
the camera model provided with the Mars 2020 images, then a test series was done on
the terrain made of sand in the ONL. The project can therefore be divided into two main
parts; mapping from cameras and estimation of pose based on the available map and
image from a camera, with the former being relevant for only the ONL. For the practicality
of the project, then the last part of pose estimation was first demonstrated on an existing
dataset; Mars 2020. After the overall functionality was proven, then the mapping algorithm
was made for testing in ONL.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
1
==PAGE:18==
Due to the complexity of this project, some limits and simplifications were set from the
beginning in order to narrow the scope of the project:
• Implement in MATLAB to simplify the development process, instead of better per­
forming C++ for instance
• Use feature matching, which is a part of the MATLAB tools
• Algorithms are not directly tailored to different lighting conditions
• Reference systems for the Mars 2020 can be transformed between MCMF and the
epirectangular projection used by the maps, as they are done in the code.
• Spectral response of the cameras are not included in this study
• Surfaces should be represented by the maps, meaning there shouldn’t be any no­
table changes

Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies

Chapter 2. Scene Analysis and the previous missions
2
Scene Analysis and the previous
missions
In order to make sense of the thesis, then the general scenes, of which the imaging sys­
tems are used in, are explained and examined here. For this thesis, then different scenes
have been used. The main scenes have been the Mars 2020 lander and simulated plan­
etary surface with craters. The changing scenes have been used to test how flexible the
developed algorithm is. The simulated planetary surface has also brought up the topic of
generating a reference map by using an imaging system, which has been shown to be
more complicated to get right than just a quick stitching of images, but this complication
is discussed later in the relevant chapter.
Before proceeding to the cases, then the definition of DEM should be stated. A DEM is
a model of the elevations of a bare surface, which is basically a 3D model of the surface
without any man­made buildings, roads or bridges and similar, and neither vegetation.
This however can sometimes however also be denoted as DTM. During this thesis, the
surface that has been worked on consist of only the bare terrain with maybe some rocks,
therefore both terms can be used. There is however an exception with the surface for the
tests, as there is an astronaut figure, but this is an area of exception. So throughout this
report, then DEM will be used for the case of Mars 2020 and DTM will be used for the
tests made in ONL.
2.1
Mars 2020
The mission called Mars 2020, which is also called the Perseverance rover, landed the
18th of February 2021 at the Jezero Crater on Mars.[2] In the context of this thesis, then
the rover had advanced its landing capabilities such that the landing ellipse, of which it was
targeting for a landing site which was much smaller than other missions. The size of the
ellipse can be seen in Figure 2.1, where some of the previous missions landing ellipses
are also shown for scale, where there is a clear difference. It should be noted that the
missions had different requirements and different landing locations on Mars. When the
landing site was chosen, then Jezero Crater was deemed as one of the dangerous sites to
land at due to the cliffs, inescapable dune fields and rocks, but on the other hand provide
more scientific value. This meant that the onboard landing navigation had to be improved
to an unprecedented level of accuracy for landing on Mars. The system is discussed in
more details in the appendix section D.2, but it achieved to reduce a position estimation
error of up to 3.2 km down to just under 40 m in 6 s. And actually managed to reduce the
error down to 5 m from the intended landing site, which is very impressive. Furthermore,
this system was used to look for where to put down the rover by using the onboard hazard
map as part of the STS. [1]
2.1.1
Camera system
The camera used by the LVS is based on heritage from designs of Mars Descent Imager
(MARDI), which was used by the MSL rover. The camera is called LCAM, which is only
used during the descent/landing phase, since the interface Field­Programmable Gate Ar­
ray (FPGA) is switched to process and use another set of stereo cameras after landing.
[4]
Another important thing to note about the LCAM, is that the camera model used to describe
the intrinsic, extrinsic and lens distortions is called CAHVORE, which is explained later in
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
3
==PAGE:20==
2.1. Mars 2020
Figure 2.1: Landing ellipses of Mars 2020 rover. Credit: National Aeronautics and Space
Administration (NASA)/JPL­Caltech, [3]
more details with the application of it. But since the Field of View (FOV) is 90°, then this
means that the camera has a fish­eye lens, where it can not be described correctly by the
simple pinhole camera, as the radial distortion is highly non­linear. For the images, then
there exist three categories, or product levels, ECM, Experiment Data Record (EDR) and
Fundamental Data Record (FDR). ECM is the lowest level product and therefore the raw
image. Normally, this would also include compression from 12­bit to 8­bit for transmission
from the spacecraft to Earth. However, since the actual bit­depth of LCAM is natively
8­bit, then there is no actual compression. EDR are the images after converting back
to 12­bit using a lookup table. FDR is the final product which has been radiometrically
calibrated from the EDR, and therefore the recommended data product to use according
to David Mayer. He also confirmed that the three products level in this case are equal, but
the EDR is 16­bit, but the image data hasn’t been stretched to fill the entire 16­bit worth
of range.1 The images are available in the Planetary Data System (PDS) data portal[5],
where they can either be downloaded manually, or something like wget can be used. In
the data portal, there are thumbnail images as .png, or in the better format .IMG, where
the header with camera parameters (calibration data) is included as well. An example of
the image can be seen in Figure 2.8a.
When looking at the histogram, Figure 2.8b, of the image shown in Figure 2.8a, then it
can be seen that there is some quantization. This is most likely due to some onboard
processing or stretching to fit the entire range. This will be handled later in the algorithm,
but basically this can be reduced by applying a Gauss­filter, which is normally used to
reduce noise.

2.1.2
Reference map and image
The LVS system relied on matching landmarks with an onboard map, including elevations.
The reference image can be seen in Figure 2.3a and have the size of 1512×1596, where
each pixel corresponds to 6m/pixel, which results in an area of 965.3 km2. The bit depth is
8 bit, corresponding to values between 0­255, this is reasoned in the fact that the native bit
depth for the LCAM system is 8­bit It should be noted that this reference image is a Surface
Reflectance Map (SRM), meaning that the surface properties such as the reflectance is
shown. This method is more robust to different lighting conditions, which however requires
that the camera is calibrated as well, which is fortunately the case of the LCAM, where
it’s a grey­scale camera. Normal true colour cameras may also work with this reference
image, however compared to the greyscale camera, then the colours isn’t used for this
purpose, so the filtration in the different colours results in lose of useful data.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
5
==PAGE:22==
2.1. Mars 2020
The onboard DEM can be seen in Figure 2.3b, where the size is equal to that of the ref­
erence image, but the pixel scale is 20m/pixel and not 6m/pixel. This difference shouldn’t
have a noticeable impact on the results, as it can be assumed that the terrain is rather
smooth. So for the usage in the developed algorithm, then the height was interpolated to
get an estimate from the estimated xy­coordinate. There exist more fine DEM, but it can
be expected that the usage of this in a real space mission requires a lot more computing
power and memory, as the values based on the single precision binary (32 bit), which
means that if it was at the same scale, then the memory needed to allocate for the map is
4 times bigger than the reference image. Figure 2.3c shows the DEM with contours. This
figure can help to get an overview of how the terrain is formed by looking at the isolines,
where each line represents a constant height. And the denser the lines, the higher slope.

Due to memory and computational limitations of the system, then the reference image was
cropped according to the estimated/predicted position and pose at the time of the image.
This resulted in a 2048x2048 image, which was then used for the landmark matching.

2.3
Simulated surface
The other dataset used, is based on a simulated planetary surface made in a sandbox in
Optical Navigation Lab (ONL) at the basement of DTU Space building 327/328. This sur­
face is characterized by sand grains which forms structures such as craters, hills, valleys
and ridges in the terrain, however it should be noted that the individual sand grains are
visible in the images, which can be a source of problems that will be discussed later. For
illumination of the terrain, then either the background light from the room can be used,
or a spotlight from an angle can be used. The background light will make the colour bal­
ance of the images warmer, which actual looks more like Mars. Furthermore, then the
background light will also provide softer light which makes the shadows ’softer’, where
the spotlight will provide a directional light which enhances the contrast of shadows.
2.3.1
Camera system
For the camera system, then two Canon 2000D DSLR cameras were used in a stereo
camera setup, which is can be seen in Figure 2.6. This was due to the requirement of
having a reference map and image, where two cameras will provide good depth data. The
focal length was generally set to 18 mm, but both were calibrated prior to the data series,
where a fixed focus was selected by moving the camera onto a smaller test pattern at a
certain height, which will be used to map the surface. After the mapping process, then the
first (left) camera is used to simulate a lander or test camera, as the map is built with the
reference being the left camera the is assumed to be at the given coordinates of the robotic
arm. Generally, any camera would work for the project, however having high resolution
will potentially yield a more fine gridded map in the end. The camera’s was likewise also
supplied with DC power directly in order to image for longer times without taking the stereo
setup apart. The general specification of the Canon 2000D DSLR camera can be seen
below.[13] The images were taken with a focal length of 18 mm, aperture of f/5.6, and
exposure of 200 ms.
The calibration data is shown in the discussion of the data.
• Sensor: CMOS
• Pixels: 6000 pixel × 4000 pixel, around 24.1 Mpixel
• Pixel size: 3.72 µm
• Focal length: Variable, but mainly 18 mm
• Aperture: f/5.6
• Exposure: 200 ms

smoothing on the Mars 2020 images is recommended, both to reduce noise, and at the
same time reduce the quantization.

Chapter 3. Theory
3
Theory
3.1
Image capturing
A digital camera capture an image by using two main components; the lens and sensor.
In this thesis, the sensor has been defined by what has been used on Mars 2020 and
what is available for the test made in ONL. There exist two main types of sensors; CMOS
and Charged Coupled Device (CCD). CMOS is widely available and consist of onboard
amplifiers for each pixel, where the conversion to a digital image is quite fast, as every pixel
can be converted directly. CCD, on the other hand, is based on measuring the charge
of each pixel, where they can be read in order, since there is no dedicated amplifiers
for each pixel. This results in a some differences, where CMOS generally provides a
fast conversion (done in parallel) but with added noise sources (especially thermal) and
slightly lower light capturing area per pixel, and CCD performs quite better with noise,
but with the disadvantage of being slower (done in serial). Another benefit of choosing a
CMOS, is that having a CCD, means that every pixel can leak or overflow charges onto
neighbouring pixels, resulting in a blooming effect. Another factor to take into account,
is the gain settings for the conversion. This is also typically called ISO, where a high
gain can amplify faint details, however this also amplify noise and lead to an increase in
thermal noise. To add more to the image capturing process then in addition to the ISO,
a change in aperture (can change the exposure and depth of field) or exposure time can
change the general exposure of the image, which means that these three settings are
important to balance.
3.2
Camera models
To describe any camera or imaging system, then a camera model is used. This model
is often the pinhole camera model, which is the simplest, as it’s straightforward to use,
however, more advanced models exist for special systems like fish eye lenses for which
the common pinhole camera model is insufficient. The parameters describing the entire
camera system can be separated into two parts; intrinsic and extrinsic parameters. The
intrinsic parameters involve the full description of the camera internally, which are the
focal length, principal point, skewness of the sensor and lastly which isn’t part of main
parameters, the sensor size and pixel size. The distortion coefficients are also part of
the intern of the camera, however, these are mostly corrected either on the whole image
or on specific pixels. The extrinsic parameters are, as the name suggest, the external
parameters, namely the orientation and position of the camera system relative to an ex­
ternal reference frame. Normally, the intrinsic parameters are fixed for a specific camera
system when the focal length and focus remain constant, where the extrinsic parameters
change as the camera system is moved or rotated in different direction.
3.2.1
Homogeneous and inhomogeneous coordinates
Before proceeding to the pinhole model, then it can be quite beneficial to introduce the
concept of homogeneous and inhomogeneous coordinates. Using homogeneous coordi­
nates for the image points, simplifies the matrix calculations, as this would then be simple
matrix multiplications with vectors. The conversion from inhomogeneous (rinhomogeneous)
to homogeneous coordinates (rhomogeneous) is done by adding an extra dimension to the
coordinate vector (which can be noted as λ), as shown in Equation 3.1[14, slide 44]. rih1,

3.2.2
The pinhole model
As stated previously, the pinhole camera is rather simple and intuitive to understand, which
is illustrated in Figure 3.1. The model is based on a true pinhole camera, however due to
the nature of such a camera (limited light means longer exposure), then camera systems
use lenses to increase the amount of captured light by having a higher aperture (the
effective opening), with the downside being the introduction of lens distortions. However,
in this case, then the basics of the pinhole camera can still be used. In the case of a lens,
then the focal length is shifted, such that the FOV is either increased or decreased. In
the case of Figure 3.3, then the FOV of the lens (blue) is decreased as the virtual focal
point (blue crossing lines) is further from the camera without the lens (red). The black
lines illustrate the effect of the lens, as the light rays may not be parallel from the left side
(world frame) of the lens to the right (camera frame). The benefits of having lenses is
mainly to increase the light incoming as the aperture can be changed, since the lenses
correct for this, and the system can be quite smaller, as the distance between the lens and
sensor can be fixed, where the individual lens elements changes the focal length. Firstly,
a point in the world reference system is chosen and projected onto the image plane.

are transformed into the image coordinates. Generally there is only a single focal length
in mm, F, and this is then converted into the two focal lengths in pixels by dividing F by
the size of the pixel in the x and y direction as shown in Equation 3.6.[16]

s = fx tan α
(3.8)
For the rotation matrix R, then it’s a simple rotation matrix which can be converted back
and forth to Euler angles (typically rotation order as z,y,x). The size of this matrix is 3x3.
The translation vector t of size 1x3 is the position of the camera relative to the world ref­
erence frame. The equivalent extrinsic matrix becomes a 3x4 matrix, and by using these
mentioned sizes of the intrinsic and extrinsic matrices, then it can be seen that the cam­
era projection matrix P have the size of 3x4. This fits the equation in Equation 3.3, where
the four­dimensional homogeneous world coordinates (3D point) is practical reduced in
dimensions to three­dimensional homogenous image coordinates (2D point). It can be
seen from the equation that the operations are quite simple as they are just matrix multi­
plications, which means that the conversion is efficient. On the other hand, then it can be
seen that radial distortions isn’t included in the matrices, which then have to be done in
addition of the matrix multiplications.
3.2.3
Distortions and other effects from using lenses
Since the camera system contains real lenses, then there can be a number of different
effects by using lenses.[14] The task of lenses is to focus incoming light onto the imaging
sensor, however, since lens material, shape and placement relative to the sensor is not
perfectly ideal, then the result might deviate. For normal lenses, then the most typical
lens effect is radial distortion, where rays of light bend more than near the optical axis.
This relates typical to how the lens was made and the quality standard. Since lenses
also depend on wavelengths (the refractive index), where the diffractions of light should
be fairly consistent through the intended light spectrum, which reduces the chromatic
aberrations. Another effect is spherical aberrations, where focus is not at the same spot.
This can for instance be cheap magnifying glass, where the focus is not at the same
distance throughout the field of view. Another effect is astigmatism, where the focus is
different between horizontal and vertical direction. There are also effects like comatic
aberration types, where points­sources off the optical axis are imaged as points with a trail
like a comet. Another effect related to the lens system, is vignetting, where the brightness
at the outer edges is more dimmed than at the centre of the image. This can be due
to the lens design or simple dimensional obstructions from the lens parts. However, the
lens system is not the only source of distortions, there are also tangential distortions that
can arise from the misalignment between the sensor and lens, where the sensor can be
tilted relative to the optical axis of the lens system. There will be a high possibility of
it appearing (at least very, very small) on a camera system like DSLR and other non­
scientific with changeable (either focal distance or entirely) lenses, since the alignment
of the lens and sensor is typical done to make it convenient for changing lens systems
where there are extra tolerances to not be impossible to take apart.
Generally, distortion is more severe for larger FOV, as the angles of the rays are larger,
however, there are typically more lenses that corrects these distortions such that they are
reduced in addition to other aberrations like chromatic aberrations.
14
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 3. Theory
Object
Image Sensor
Object
Figure 3.3: The pinhole model of a camera combined with a lens, where the blue lines
indicate the equivalent focal length of the camera with the lens
Distortions like radial distortion and tangential distortions, including intrinsic parameters,
can typical be found by calibration and then be undistorted. Effects like chromatic aber­
rations are more difficult to handle, as it depends on the specific wavelengths and image
sensor combined, typical with a Bayer filter for colour imaging. So these effects are typical
corrected and reduced as much as possible during the manufacturing of the lens, as they
are almost impossible to correct afterwards.
Radial distortion
Radial distortion can be modelled in different ways, but most models are using the distance
from the optical centre of the imaging system, which ideally should be at the centre of
the sensor. The most common model, which for instance MATLAB and OpenCV uses,
is the polynomial model.[16] This model is shown in Equation 3.9, where x, y are the
undistorted normalized image coordinates (normalized pixel coordinates with the optical
centre as origo), k1, k2, k3 are the radial distortion coefficients, r is the Euclidean distance
to the optical centre(r2 = x2 + y2), and finally xdistorted, ydistorted are the distorted image
coordinates. It should be noted that this model with only even terms, is just one, as several
others with odd terms, and all terms exists.
xdistorted = x
(
1 + k1 r2 + k2 r4 + k3 r6)
ydistorted = x
(
1 + k1 r2 + k2 r4 + k3 r6)
(3.9)
This polynomial can be extended with more terms, however, this would rarely be needed.
When the imaging system is expected to have low distortions, then two coefficients can

be sufficient and therefore removal of the third term can simplify the image processing
slightly. The radial distortion model shown in Equation 3.9 is also visualized in Figure 3.4,
where the coefficients are written as well. For radial distortion, there exist two main types
of distortions; positive and negative, also known as pincushion and barrel distortions.
Here is only a single coefficient used for the purpose of demonstrating the two types. It
can be seen visually from the two figures that there is a clear difference between having a
positive or negative displacements. When the magnification increases with distance from
the optical centre, as shown with the area which is larger at the edge than at the centre in
Figure 3.4a, then it’s pincushion distortion. On the other hand, when the magnification de­
creases with distance from the optical centre, as in Figure 3.4b, then it’s barrel distortion.
Another way of seeing this, is that fish eye lenses tend to have best details at the centre,
where objects near the edges get more distorted but are used to get a very broad view.
This effect can then be described by using these radial distortion terms. The distortion
can quickly be identified if there is supposed to be a line (which are not tangential, as the
name implies) which is straight or a series of objects which lines up near the edges. If this
line or line of objects is bent either toward or away from the optical centre, then it can be
compared to the figures in 3.4, to get an idea of what type of distortion it is.

Tangential Distortion
In addition to radial distortion, tangential distortion is a kind of distortion which is solely
about the alignment of the individual lens elements and the sensor to the optical axis,
which therefore all contribute to small misalignments which results in a distortion. This can
once again be modelled differently depending on which model that is used. In MATLAB,
then the model used is shown in Equation 3.10, where the notation follows that from 3.9
regarding x, y, r, xdistorted and ydistorted , and the two tangential distortion coefficients p1
and p2.[16]
xdistorted = x +
(
2 p1 x y + p2 (r2 + 2 x2)
)
ydistorted = y +
(
p1 (r2 + 2 y2) + 2 p2 x y
)
(3.10)
The tangential distortion is likewise also illustrated similar to the radial distortion, where
Equation 3.10 are used on a grid of pixels forming an object to be imaged. This results in

Figure 3.5a, where it can be seen there is a clear tilt of the image object. This tilt doesn’t

from the two axes, and therefore symmetric about this tilted axis. In Figure 3.5b, there is
only a single non­zero coefficient, which means that the axis of symmetry is aligned in one
direction (parallel to the y­axis at x=0). This case is also exaggerated to show the effect,
but most imaging systems are well aligned and therefore very small tangential distortion.

the entire lens is aligned, but in reality the lens system most likely would also have some
effect, but overall this case is exaggerated to show what effect it has. Then the two sensors
are shown in Figure 3.6c, where the black lines correspond to the traced light rays and
therefore the pixel position. This figure shows the situation similar to Figure 3.5b, where

3.2.4
Calibrations and camera models reference
To reduce the effects of distortions, then imaging systems (camera/image sensor and
lens) can be calibrated. The calibration can be done differently depending on the specific
use case. This can either be a chessboard pattern where images are captured at different
positions and orientations. In this case, then other patterns can also be used for better
calibration. Most calibration routines require that the pattern is a flat plane. Here, it’s
important to know the scale of the pattern in order for the calibration to achieve a good
quality.This type of calibration can be done more consistent with the support of a robotic
arm. Every calibration require that the focus and focal length remains constant, as this
impacts the calibration done. However, one thing that should be noted regarding this

calibration type, is that the camera is not calibrated in regard to the spectral response
of the sensor neither whatever there are ’dead’ or ’hot’ pixels, so this can be done when
having references, like a colour palette. Mars 2020 rover had calibration targets too in
order to get accurate colours in the images.[17] For stereocameras, then the calibration
can easily be done as well, where the relative orientation and position between the two
cameras can be found when knowing that a calibration pattern is fixed in space.
After the calibration has been done, then the output can be used directly for image pro­
cessing like mapping. However, NASA/JPL uses another kind of representation than
the standard pinhole camera model, namely the CAHVOR model, or in the simplest
form CAHVOR.[18] This model(CAHVOR) represents the different parameters as vec­
tors, where the radial distortion isn’t included. The CAHVOR model will be explained in
more details later.
Fish eye lenses
Since LCAM of Mars 2020 used a fish eye lens, then it’s relevant to see how these lenses
can be modelled. Fish eye lenses, a special type of lenses, where the standard pin­hole
camera is insufficient to describe the imaging system. This is due to the high FOV and
bending of the light rays to much higher degree. It would result in an extremely high radial
distortion, and correction of only radial distortion wouldn’t be enough to correct for this
fish eye effect. To correct for this fish eye distortion, then changes to the camera model
must be made. In this regard, there exist different methods to do this. Mars 2020 used
an extended CAHVOR model by including an extra parameter with models the entrance
18
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3. Theory
pupil, however these are more or less just to differentiate between which version and
type that is used). This forms the CAHVORE model, which models the fish eye effect as
a moving entrance pupil.[19] This effect is nonlinear, which is far more complex than just
transforming the standard CAHVOR to photogrammetric parameters. For the Mars 2020
case, then this extension was used for the stereo cameras and LCAM. The extension
was used such that the LCAM images were warped to remove the radial distortion and
then projected onto the tangent plane of map1, meaning that there was no direct undis­
tortion. The complexity of using this model to undistore the images can be quite a task
to complete, therefore this is typical not used for other cases. In MATLAB, then the cali­
bration for fish eye lenses uses the Scaramuzza model[16], and stretching and distortion
parameters. This models the fish eye effect by, instead of having a plane, then using a
plane similar to a parabola. This method then uses a polynomial to describe this parabola.
citeScaramuzza2006AMotion[20]
The CAHVOR camera model
The CAHVOR model consist of the following parameters;[19][18]
• C: Camera Center vector, or in other words; coordinates in a given world reference
frame
• A: Camera Axis in a given world reference frame
• H: Horizontal information vector
• V: Vertical information vector
• O: The Optical axis, value is close to A
• R: Radial lens distortion coefficients
For converting into the photogrammetric model parameters like focal length and principal
point, then this will not be showed. All the conversion comes from [18], where the opposite
conversion can be seen as well, but these haven’t been used in this thesis.At first, then
optical centre of the image is found. This is done by taking the dot­product of the camera
axis, A, and the respective horizontal and vertical information vectors, H and V, which is
shown in Equation 3.11[18, eq. 1c, 1d]
hc = A · H
vc = A · V
(3.11)
The focal length can be found as the length(||||) of the cross product between the camera
axis, A, and the respective horizontal and vertical information vectors, H and V. This is
shown in Equation 3.12[18, eq. 1e, 1f].
hs = ||A × H||
vs = ||A × V||
(3.12)

3.3
Filters ­ Image processing
Probably the most fundamental part of image processing, is the usage of filters on images.
These filters can typical be used as kernels, which describes their functionality, and they
are mostly used on greyscale images, since the kernels are convoluted or correlated with
a moving window. They can of course be used separately on multi­band images like red,
green and blue bands. Examples uses of filters involves sharpening, blurring, edge/corner
detection, gradient, low/high pass, median and many more. They can also be combined,
however then the combination is done at the same data, meaning a blurring followed by
edge detection can not be combined into a single kernel, since it will be a mix of both filters,
where the nearby pixel should maybe also be blurred prior to do the edge detection. In
this thesis project, then the following filters has been used, either as a trial for usage or
direct part of the developed algorithm.
3.3.1
Gradient
Gradient filters are typical used for finding edges, corners or points in an image. This is
epically useful for finding features defining clear boundaries and edges etc. However, this
can also be useful for other cases than images as the gradients can be applied to elevation
maps to discover the slope of the terrain, however, one should be careful with the chose of
kernel since they might not be practical for having the correct scaling units. Below are the
Sobel kernels showed in addition of a custom kernel for using with the DEM/DTM. Other
exist, but it was seen from the start of the project that the main application was finding the
direction and magnitude, and Sobel are very good at this.
Sobel
One of the most common gradient kernels, are the Sobel kernels. The kernels come
in a horizontal(x) and vertical(y) variants(see Equation 3.22 and 3.23).[21, slide 18] The

3.3.2
Blurring ­ noise reduction
To reduce noise in an image, then different kernels can be used. Either simple kernels
with uses an average, kernels which uses gauss smoothing or kernels which puts more
weight on central pixels. These kernels can typical be used at different sizes from 3x3 to
very large kernels depending on the specific need.
Gauss
Gauss smoothing kernel uses a gaussian function, as shown in Equation 3.30.[22, slide
84] This function can be used to define a kernel. This is simply done by defining the size
of the kernel, the standard deviation σ, and then have the x and y are the pixel distance
along the horizontal and vertical directions. This will form the kernel h, where it should be
normalized by it’s sum.
h(x, y) =
1
2 πσ2 e−x2+y2
2 σ2
(3.30)
Average
The averaging filters typical follows a very standard template, where the cases from these
templates involves putting a weight on central pixels. The template follows that all ele­
ments of a kernel of any size have the same value, which can be done easily by setting all
to one and divide with the sum of all the elements. This division, normalizes the matrix,
meaning that the general intensity doesn’t increase, but rather gets averaged. This nor­
malization can be very important for keeping the same dynamic range of the image. The
kernel for this operation can be seen in Equation 3.31, where all elements are equal.[22,
slide 80]
Gaverage =

centre pixel, which enhances the contrast.
3.4
Features
Features in images can be separated into different categories depending on which feature
types they can be used for. There are feature types for that can be used on different
feature types. An example of good feature descriptors for corner points are the FAST
and Harris features. Harris features detect corners by finding the gradient of the images
along the horizontal and vertical directions, and then smoothen the gradient components
(circular gaussian 5x5 in MATLAB). Then a score is computed for the strength of the
corner, such that it can be used to select how strongly a corner point is. This type of
algorithm of finding the features can be seen as using the intersection of edges found from
the gradients. FAST on the other hand, looks at each potential corner point and tests if
there are pixels in a circular area that makes the potential corner point an actual corner.
FAST requires far less computing resources than Harris, however, the more systematic
algorithm of Harris can be beneficial in some cases. [23] Then there are features which
can be used for blobs in an image, these can be SIFT, SURF and KAZE. SIFT and SURF
is partly the same. SIFT rescales the difference of gaussians[24], where SURF uses the
integral and laplacian of gaussian, which are done with box filters.[25] KAZE on the other
hand works like SIFT and SURF, but the difference being that the image isn’t smoothed
with a gaussian.[26] The exact mathematical background for each of these features will
not be presented, as they are used as they are in the MATLAB functions. According
to a study by Baerjee and Mistry, [25], then SURF performs better with rotated, blurred,
warped images under different lighting conditions and faster than SIFT, but SIFT handles
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
23
==PAGE:40==
3.5. Position and orientation estimation
better, but in the end, it depends on the specific case. There are several more feature
types that are based on different properties, but these haven’t been used, or it was found
that they didn’t work for this project.
MATLAB has a good overview of the different features, where a subset are shown below,
and the different use cases can be seen.[27]
SURF &SIFT ­ Blob features:
• Multiscale detection
• Object detection and image registration with scale and rotation changes
KAZE ­ Multi­scale blob features:
• Multiscale detection
• Reduced blurring of object boundaries
FAST & Harris ­ corner features:
• Single­scale detection
• Point tracking, image registration with little or no scale change, corner detection in
scenes of human origin, such as streets and indoor scenes.
3.5
Position and orientation estimation
Position and pose estimations are vital parts of this thesis project. For this project, then
the relative position is to be estimated using vision based on a reference image and map.
In order to make this happen, then the on how a position can be estimated from an image
is needed, where it’s assumed that there is only a single camera and a reference image
and DTM or DEM.
3.5.1
Perspective­n­Point (PnP)
In the field of computer vision, then estimation of the position and pose of a camera can
be done by using the known geometry of pinhole camera model. An image point, which
has been cleaned from distortions, can be projected along a line in the 3D world refer­
ence. This is as well the case for any of the other image points. However, one essential
thing is that there is missing key information here, and that’s the depth, or distance on this
projected line. This is where feature matching between a reference image (with known 3D
coordinates) and the given image can be used, as this will result in point correspondences
with known 3D world coordinates and 2D image coordinates. The specific pixel intensity
doesn’t matter after the correspondences has been found. This means that estimation of
position and pose of the camera can be done as the needed information has been gath­
ered; 3D world coordinates of the 2D image coordinates. A technique called PnP can be
used here to find the correct position and pose of the camera by using several of these
points. The most simple application is setting n = 3, meaning a P3P­problem is formed.
This can be solved by many different methods. One of the intuitive methods is Grünert’s
solution, which was published back in 1841. This method can be more simple to under­
stand, as it’s based on a more geometric approach. MATLAB, however, uses a special
algorithm with outlier detection and rejection, which can be far more robust compare to
Grünert’s solution. However, to give an overall idea of how geometry of the image points
and world points can give a position and orientation, then Grünert’s solution can be used
as an example, including the Kabsch algorithm which relates the relative position of the
points to a world reference frame. However, one important note to PnP, is that it requires
that the points are evenly distributed in order to achieve a good estimate of the pose of
24
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 3. Theory
the camera. This can be explained by imaging the worst case of all matches being within
10 m of each other and the lander being 2 km above the points. In this case, it might end
up being perceived to be a single larger point, which will not provide useful information
as the geometry is almost like a 1D case, and the whole PnP geometry falls apart. If the
points are at a line, then the uncertainty along this line will decrease (better estimated),
while the uncertainty for any other directions will be high (worse estimate). Thus, it’s
important to have points which are spread evenly, and higher number will generally be
beneficial. This is also well known problem in the field of GNSS, where a good spread
of the satellites reduces the Dilution of Position (DOP) and therefore also the uncertainty.
There exist several different dilution parameters for different directions, such as horizontal
and vertical, however this will not be discussed in this report.2
Grünert’s solution
Grünert’s solution is assuming that there are 3 known points. These 3 points and the cam­
era forms a tetrahedron, where the known parts of this tetrahedron are the baselengths a,
b, c (Euclidean distance between the points x1, x2 and x3) between each of the 3 points
and the angles(α, β and γ) between the points as seen from the camera (this requires
that the camera intrinsic is known). The possible tetrahedrons can be seen in Figure 3.7,
where the different solutions can be seen.
Figure 3.7: The four different possible solutions to a P3P problem. [15, slide 26] also from
[28, slide 32]
The 3 base lengths a, b and c and angles α, β, γ can be used in order to find expressions
for the 3 unknown sidelengths. The baselengths can be written as in Equation 3.32.[15,
slide 21]
a = |X3 −X2|
b = |X3 −X3|
c = |X2 −X1|
(3.32)
And the angles can be found from the image points as in Equation 3.33[15, slide 21],
where x1, x2 and x3 are homogenous image coordinates of the points, corrected with
the intrinsic matrix as in Equation 3.34(xapparenti are the pixel coordinates). The image
coordinates x1 can also be looked at as vectors from the centre of the camera system,

However, one can easily see a problem when solving for v in Equation 3.38, as it results
in 4 solutions (due to the 4th order), this will result in 4 different sets of sidelengths. This
has been worked on for many years and different methods to find the correct solution has
been published since 1841. There are much more complex methods, where for example
a method from Gao are used in MATLAB[29][30], however to simplify this process, then
a fourth point can be used to see which solution is most correct. However, this method
is very sensitive to noise and outliers as it then depends on only four points from all the
available points, which means that this not the most accurate way. But to simplify this
method, then one can look at the different cases of the tetrahedron, where the sidelengths
are different but the angles (between points as seen from the camera) and base length
is the same. It can be seen that if one can get an estimate of the correct sidelength to
a single point, then it’s solved. This is where the fourth point can be used in order to
get another set of solution and looking at what solution match best between the original
and extra set of solutions. MATLAB however solves the P3P problem differently such
that it uses a Random Sample Consensus (RANSAC) routine while searching for the best
solution.
3.5.2
Kabsch algorithm
In order to find the position and orientation of the camera from the found sidelengths, then
the Kabsch algorithm can be used. This algorithm uses least­squares fitting between two
datasets of 3D coordinates. Since the image coordinates are natively 2D(3 homogenous
coordinates), then these can be converted into unit vectors such that the direction of the
light rays can be traced where it will intersect with the 3D world points. These unit vectors
can then be scaled with the found sidelengths to get the position of the points relative to
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
27
==PAGE:44==
3.6. Stereo vision
the camera, Xc. The relation between the camera reference frame and world reference
frame can be seen in Equation 3.40, where the points in the world reference frame, Xw,
can be transformed into the image reference frame, Xc, by a rotation R and a translation
t.[15, slide 27] There are typical also some noise which can be added to this equation as
a noise vector, but this is not included here.
Xc = R Xw + t
(3.40)
It can be seen from Equation 3.40 that there are two unknown variables in this equation,
which results in a total of 6 unknown parameters(3 rotation angles and 3 translation direc­
tions). In order to estimate these, then his can be done by using the Kabsch algorithm.
This algorithm follows the following steps:[15, slide 28 and 29]
1. Calculation of the centroids (the average point) of both sets of 3D points
2. Form new 3D point sets by subtracting the average/centroid from the corresponding
sets of 3D points. This will result in having zero translation and leaving rotation
matrix being the unknown.
3. Define a H matrix, such that it’s the sum of the products of the two new 3D point
sets
4. Use singular value decomposition(SVD) to get the singular values
5. Then the sign of the determinant of V UT from SVD can be used to find the rotation
matrix R. When the determinant is equal to ­1, then it’s typical a sign of noisy data
and a case of reflection, where V can be multiplied with ­1 in order to get a realistic
result.
6. The rotation matrix R can then ve found from V UT where the sign can of the deter­
minant can be used as well.
7. The translation can be found from Equation 3.40, when replacing the coordinates of
the points with the centroids of Xc and Xw
8. Finally the camera centre in the world reference frame can be found using Equa­
tion 3.40, and setting the point Xc to zero (this is the camera centre in the image
reference frame).
The specific steps can be seen in the appendix (subsection D.1.1), but by following these
steps, then the camera position and orientation in the world reference frame can easily be
found from the original P3P problem (actual P3P + 1 point). The major downside of this
method is the amount of noise that needs to be addressed. As it’s mentioned in the paper
from Arun et al. [31], then RANSAC can be a clever move on addressing this problem by
detecting and rejecting the outliers which introduces most noise. This is where the built­in
functions from MATLAB have a clear advantage, as they include this optimization as well.
3.6
Stereo vision
3.6.1
The basic model
A stereo camera consist of two cameras, which are positioned and orientated such that
part of the image views overlaps, but not from the same view. There are three configura­
tions of the stereo camera; parallel, convergent and divergent. Parallel stereo cameras is
like the name applies point in the same direction. Convergent, means that the optical axes
of the views intersects at a point, or in other words point towards each other. Divergent
stereo cameras is the opposite of the convergent meaning that the optical axes points
28
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3. Theory
away from each other. The most used and simple setup is having the cameras parallel.
When using stereo camera, then the depth information can be efficiently extracted from
the disparity. The disparity can be found from the similar triangles ((pl, P, pr), (Ol, P, Or)
), where the relation between the baselengths and heights can be seen in Equation 3.41,
and Figure 3.8.[32, slide 34] This figure is related to the epipolar geometry, however, as­
suming that the corresponding points has been found already, then this can be simplified
in this figure.
Figure 3.8: Depth perception of parallel stereo cameras from [32, slide 34], with credit to
Kristen Garuman
T + xl −xr
Z −f
= T
Z
(3.41)
Z can be isolated as shown in Equation 3.42.[32, slide 34] This equation can be used
to get an estimate on how the disparity (between xl and xr) affects the depth at certain
focal lengths. One thing to note about this is that the camera should be calibrated such
that the points can be traced to the correct 3D point. Another thing that should be noted
with this is that the disparity is discrete, which means that there are only equivalents to
1, 2.. pixels. An interesting thing about Equation 3.42 is that by increasing the focal length,
base length, then the depth perception increases. Furthermore, it can be seen that the
depth of a point is inversely proportional to the disparity, meaning that a point at infinity
has a disparity of 0.
Z = f
T
xr −xl
(3.42)
This model is visualized in Figure 3.9, where it can be seen that when the pixel disparity is
1, then the depth is significant higher than for when the disparity is 5 pixels. When looking
at the colouring scale, then it can also be seen that making the disparity 5 times bigger,
then the depth information at same baselines and focal length is 5 times smaller, which
confirms Equation 3.42. Since the focal length and FOV are closely related (Equation 3.7),
then this has also been shown to add more intuitive understanding. It can be clearly
seen that increasing the focal length (decreasing the FOV) generally increases the depth
perception, where it’s similar to the trend of the baseline. In realistic cases, then it’s
optimal to select a threshold of few pixels as disparity, as there may be some sources of
noise and error in the calibration which can move a point to some degree. This will be
discussed later if this has any use case for space missions.
The mentioned method of finding the position and orientation of the camera requires that
the 3D world points are known, however, this can also be done in the other way such

that 3D points are found relative to the camera. Normally a single image from a single
camera can estimate some geometry, but there will be a scale ambiguity meaning that
the relative distance to points is unknown. In order to remove this scale ambiguity, then
a stereo camera can be used as this will provide two view points of the same points, and
therefore provide a geometric solution. Other methods would be to have two or more im­
ages where the position and orientation of the camera is known. Stereo cameras consist
of two cameras, which has been calibrated such that the relative position and orientation
difference between the two cameras is known. This yield a special geometry, which will
now be explained.
The geometric argument for why a single camera with a single view can not estimate a
pose comes from the limitation of only having a 2D point which can give a line from the
camera into the 3D space, but here, the distance from the camera to the actual point is
unknown, as it’s only known that the point must be along this line. This is solved by in­
troducing two camera views, where a point can be assumed to be present in both views.
One interesting adaption is to use a virtual or artificial stereo camera, meaning that the
position and orientation of a camera at different locations can be used, however results
30
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3. Theory
might not be as great due to uncertainties in the position and orientation of the camera
compared to a calibrated stereo setup. This is typical of the method of pose estimation
by comparing features from a reference image with known coordinates with a calibrated
camera, however due to the uncertainties of the pose of the camera, then additional sen­
sors like Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU) would be beneficial in a Extended Kalman Filter
(EKF), which Mars 2020 used.
3.6.2
Epipolar geometry
One of the most important parts of stereo cameras is the geometry. When a 3D point can
be seen by two camera views with a known relative distance between the two camera
views, then three vectors can be defined, such that there is a line from view 1 to the point,
view 2 to the point, and finally view 1 to view 2 (the baseline). These three vectors spans
a plane, which is called the epipolar plane (π). The epipolar geometry can be seen in
Figure 3.10[32, slide 16], where the camera centres are noted as c, c′, and the point which
is seen in both images, X. This plane is characteristic by epipoles on both images (e, e′),
where the baseline between the two camera views intersects with the respective image
planes. These are also the vanishing points for the epipolar lines. The epipolar lines
(l, l′) is the line which intersects the epipolar plane and image planes, which all intersects
the epipole. These epipolar lines are very useful for finding corresponding points in two
images, as the search space is reduced from 2D (anywhere in an image) to 1D (along the
epipolar line).
Associated to the epipolar geometry, then the fundamental and essential matrices can be
used in addition to this, as they can be used for finding these epipolar lines in a second
view from points in the first view.[32]
Figure 3.10: Epipolar geometry with the epipolar plane to the left and the epipolar lines
and epipoles in the figure to the right. Taken from [33] like [32, slide 16]
Essential matrix
The essential matrix, E, have 5 degrees of freedom in addition to a scale ambiqity. The
epipolar lines can be found from Equation 3.43[32, slide 16], x or x′ are the respective
points in the images from the 3D point X.
When finding the essential matrix, then different methods exist such as 8­point algorithm
from Longuet­Higgins(1981), 5­point algorithm from Nistér(2003). The only requirement
for finding the essential matrix, is that the image coordinates are normalized with the in­
trinsics.[32][33]This means that the intrinsics is included prior to the essential matrix, and
therefore limits the required number of point correspondences (down to 5 point correspon­
dences).
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
31
==PAGE:48==
3.7. Reference frames and transformations
l′ = ET x
l = E x′
(3.43)
Fundamental matrix
Due to the requirement of having normalized image coordinates, then there is also a matrix
which used the image coordinates as they are, with the downside of requiring additional
point correspondences. The fundamental matrix, F, is however related to the essential
matrix, E, as shown in Equation 3.44, where K′, is the intrinsic matrix of the camera to the
right (in the figure), and K is the intrinsics of the other camera. [32, slide 19]
F = K′−T E K−1
(3.44)
The method used to find the fundamental matrix is requiring at least 8­point correspon­
dences, where a method like the 8­point algorithm from Longuet­Higgins(1981) can be
used.
3.7
Reference frames and transformations
Due to the usage of images from real missions, then knowing which reference frame that
they are given in is important. For this project, then a number of different reference frames
was given by the images and reference maps.
• Geographic ­ Latitude, longitude and altitude/height
• MCMF, similar to Ears Centered Ears Fixed (ECEF)
• Epirectangular projection
The epirectangular projection is defined as the tangent plane with origin from a certain
place. This is typical related to the latitude, which once again is related to MCMF. This
means that transformation between these projections can be done more easily. As exam­
ples, then Mars 2020 uses the MCMF reference frame as standard for the LCAM images,
where the DTM that has been downloaded uses the epirectangular projection. The Mars
2020 rover does however also use geographic coordinates for showing features on maps.
Therefore, this transformation between these are needed. For the tests in the ONL, then
the reference system provided by the robot has been used, which was based on a carte­
sian reference system. This reference frame is shown in chapter 5.
3.7.1
Transformation between latitude/longitude and epirectangular
projection
Given a set of coordinates, given in geographic representation (latitude/longitude), then
those can be transformed into the epirectangular projection by noting that that projection
is given by the tangent plane, where the latitude and longitude can be converted to this
rectangular grid. This is especially useful for images of planets, since the pixels can then
be in a rectangular grid. Below are the conversions, where x, y are the coordinates in
the epirectangular projection(cartesian), and R is the radial distance from the centre of
Earth, λ, φ are the longitude and latitude. λ0, φ0 are the central in the map, which defines
where x, y = 0, 0 are in the longitude and latitude. φ1 are the latitude where the scaling is
correct, as this project will be distorted towards the poles

3.7.2
Geocentric and geodetic latitudes
When dealing with geographic coordinates, then there are two different ways of referring to
a planet; geocentric and geodetic latitudes. It should be noted that the height/altitude and
latitude are the only components which changes, as the longitude can be assumed to be
the same due to the rotational geometry. The source of this difference is the flattening of
the spinning sphere, which forms an ellipsoid. This is illustrated in Figure 3.11[35] In order
Figure 3.11: Relation between geocentric, φ′, and geodetic, φ, latitudes
to convert between these, then the latitude is needed in addition to the flattening. For this
project, then the MATLAB functions geocentricLatitude and geodeticLatitudeFromGeo­
centric. There are other functions like geoc2geod and the reverse geod2geoc functions
which convert the height as well, but these haven’t been used.
3.7.3
Transformation between geographic and MCMF
To transform between the geographic representation and the MCMF representation, then
the commands lla2ecef and ecef2lla can be used in MATLAB, where the equatorial radius
and flattening of Mars can be used in order to use the MCMF and not ECEF. The relation
between these two can be seen in Figure 3.12.[36]

Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
4
Implementation and synthesis
This chapter will discuss the implementation of the algorithms developed in this project
and what considerations that has been behind some of the changes made underway. In
order to keep the main part of the report focused on the work done in this project, then
the description of the LVS from Mars 2020 will be in the appendix.
4.1
Overview of algorithm, and considerations
Before proceeding to the actual parts of the algorithm, then an overview of the different
parts will be presented. For this project, then two main parts has been developed, which
has been the pose estimation of the camera and step prior to this; generating a DTM and
orthophoto. It was chosen from the start that the pose estimation would be based on fea­
ture matching between the camera image and an orthophoto. This path was selected, as
Mars 2020 used the same method of feature matching, and demonstrated that it can be
used for high precision landing. However, by choosing this path, two very strict require­
ments forms as a DTM and orthophoto is required for it to work. This means that in order
to test the method on other datasets than Mars 2020, then these must be provided or
generated in order to use this pose estimation. This has resulted in having the two parts,
where the map generation algorithm has mainly been developed after the pose estimation
algorithm was formed. The reasoning behind this order of development is that it was first
tested on Mars 2020 images, to confirm that the overall idea works.
4.1.1
The workflow for using the algorithms

The coordinates from the images is added in the image headers, which has been cor­
rected/processed to be the true position after touch down, the reason to have these co­
ordinates will be explained in better details, but simply it’s for auto cropping of the map
and comparison later after the pose has been estimated. Furthermore, the DEM and
orthophoto has been provided by USGS[37], which means that the pose estimation algo­
rithm can be directly used. On the opposite side of Mars 2020, then the test setup made
in the ONL, is build on top of a specific image series with reference coordinates. How­
ever, the DTM+Orthophoto is required before testing with additional stereo images. This
results in two main algorithms developed; Pose estimation and DTM+Orthophoto gener­
ation. This will be discussed in the next couple of sections, including the considerations
to the changes made underway.
4.2
Estimation of the camera pose
The pose of the camera is essential to this thesis, as this is the main topic. It was chosen
from the beginning to follow a path which is based on feature matching, due to previous
experience within this field. Other paths could be using the correlation/convolution based
on patches like Mars 2020. The benefit of using feature matching is that essentially only
the DTM is necessary, if the reference features are already found. This ability was added
to the algorithm, as this will skip the searching for features repeatable for a larger image
series.
4.2.1
Goals
The goals for this part are rather difficult to set up before hand compared to to f.ex. Mars
2020, since it uses additional inputs other than just a camera. However, the main goal is
to have a functioning pose estimation algorithm which can give estimations which closely
matches the true pose. The exact estimation accuracy should be good enough, as it would
not be possible to reach the performance of Mars 2020 as it used both an EKF, where
propagated estimation of the position and pose from onboard IMU has been used to effi­
ciently improve the result. Thus, it was firstly considered replicating this by implementing
such an EKF, however that was quickly identified to be a far more complex task which
required more knowledge of the entire spacecraft, noise figures from the sensors etc. For
the case of Mars 2020 dataset, then it’s expected to be estimated the pose to some de­
gree, but due to simplifications and assumptions of the algorithm, then it’s expected to not
be as perfect as LVS.
On the other hand, then the test series should be far better performing compared to the
36
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
Mars 2020 data set, however, there are addition sources of error as the required DTM and
orthophoto needs to be generated as well. It’s rather difficult to predict and do a realistic
expectation, however, there should at least be within ±1° in the attitude, and hopefully
within 1 cm, but that is not based on anything special.
4.2.2
Assumptions
In order to estimate the pose, then a number of assumptions are made to simplify the
process.
• The method works with standard images, without the need of calibrating the spectral
response
• The task is not to achieve a perfect accuracy, but to implement an algorithm which
gives a good accuracy
• Lighting conditions aren’t taken into considerations, but they have an effect on the
images
• The cameras used for capturing the images are calibrated
These assumptions likewise limits the scope to be manageable to do within the project
period.

on the version. For the Mars 2020 case, then the full/binned version of the images are
not relevant as they already are binned, neither is the corrections as this step is solely for
the case of the ONL.

Figure 4.3: Overview of the pose estimation algorithm, notation follows Figure 4.2
Starting from the top, then the DTM(or in the case of Mars 2020 DEM), and orthophoto
is loaded. Then an optional step can be done by including pre­extracted features from
the orthophoto, which will reduce the computation time, this requires that the user once
again choose where the file is located and what feature type to use. After this then the
image series is loaded, which requires that the image series in addition to the coordinates
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
37
==PAGE:54==
4.2. Estimation of the camera pose
is preloaded into a structure in MATLAB. An additional optional step can be done, in order
to reduce the time significant further, which uses a pre­estimated pose to crop the map
which fits the region covered by the image. Then a lighter version of the DTM/DEM is
made that can be used to predict the intersecting point of the terrain by propagating the
line from the pre­estimated pose of the camera. This is followed by the bigger function
called EstimatePosition (V1 for Mars 2020, and V3 for ONL), where the calibration details
of the camera are needed as well. The naming was chosen as the starting point, but it
estimates the pose (position and orientation). This function manage the feature matching,
undistorting the matched points and finally estimates the pose of the camera by triangu­
lation from the DTMDEM. This function will be discussed in much further details below.
For the case of ONL, then corrections must be done, in order to correct for the skewness
of the reference frame caused by the DTM generation and the specific test setup, this is
not needed for the Mars 2020 case. After this step, then the pose estimation is done. It
should be noted that the algorithm is build on the usage of an image series, which requires
that test images must be converted into this structure. This was chosen, to have the data
input and output collected into a single variable instead of two.
4.2.4
The final method in details
As it was shown in Figure 4.3, then in order to estimate pose of a camera, certain steps
are required to be done before the actual estimation can be done. Each of these steps will
now be looked more into, including a deep dive into the actual pose estimation functions
and how this function has evolved during the project.
The initial steps
Step 1
­ Load the DTM/DEM and orthophoto/SRM
This step is rather self­explanatory, as the map is loaded. It shall be noted in the case of
Mars 2020, then it’s the DEM and SRM, and for the images in ONL, then it’s the DTM and
generated orthophoto.
Step 2
­ Load the optional pre­found features of the orthophoto
In order to speed things up, then the features of the reference image/orthophoto/SRM can
be found independently of the test images. This will typically reduce the computation time,
as detection and extraction of the features takes significant time to do. Here it’s possible
to select which feature type and settings to use, where the predefined features are shown
in Table 4.2.
Step 3
­ Load image series
Step 3 was made such that it was easier to test many different test series in one go.
It’s more optional for the Mars 2020 case, but still used. In order to use the test series,
then it requires that the images are loaded into a specific structure, as it will contain any
necessary information like coordinates, and two versions of binned or the normal images
(ONL case only). This is handled by a script, TestSeries2imdatabse, with an associated
function, RebinImage. The RebinImage function is basically just binning the images to
2 × 2, which means that squares of four pixels are combined into one larger pixel, which
normally can reduce the noise, with the impact of having less resolution. In this project,
then the goal of testing with binned images is similar to Mars 2020, where the binned
image means less computational load, as there are fewer pixels to handle.
This is done by firstly extracting two rows of the image, and then sum each column of
two pixels each (this corresponds to the first sum along the vertical axis). In order to sum
along the other direction, then the row vector of the first sum is reshaped, such that every
other summed pixel is put under the previous pixel. This will essentially make two rows
38
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
which half as many columns. Then the sum can be taken again, and then the output can
either be the sum or the average (divide the sum with 4) of the four subpixels. This is then
repeated for every image colour channel and for every set of rows.
In order to make the structure for the test series for the ONL case, then the images are
collected into folders with the names of the test series. Here it doesn’t matter with the
sorting of the left and right images into separate folders as the script is programmed such
that is sorting by the serial number of the DSLR camera, where it’s assumed that the
camera is placed at the same spot every time. There should however be a numbering
used in the file naming, such that the program assign the correct coordinates in relation
to the path of the robot. This program also uses the calibration data made with the built­in
calibration tools that is saved with folder and file naming, which is used to differentiate
between focal lengths and if it’s done for the normal or binned images. The coordinates
are made by running the CSVgenerator, which makes a file with the relevant settings to
the format the robotic arm uses.
This means that the images are loaded into a structure with the following data:
• Series name ­ Which test series that is done
• Folder ­ The folder of the image
• File name ­ The filename of the image
• Camera ID ­ The camera serial number
• Left ­ If the camera is the left view, then this is 1, otherwise 0
• Right ­ If the camera is the right view, then this is 1, otherwise 0
• Time ­ The time tag of the image
• Image(MovImage) ­ The image
• Binned Image ­ The binned version of the image
• The calibration data for the normal image
• The calibration errors for the normal image
• The calibration data for the binned image
• The calibration errors for the binned image
• Robotic arm ­ Rail position [mm]
• Robotic arm ­ x position [mm]
• Robotic arm ­ y position [mm]
• Robotic arm ­ yr position, calculated as the combined Rail and y position(Rail­y) [mm]
• Robotic arm ­ z position [mm]
• Robotic arm ­ A rotation [°]
• Robotic arm ­ B rotation [°]
• Robotic arm ­ C rotation [°]
Step 4
­ Approximated pose
In order to make the algorithm faster, then it’s a good idea to have a prediction of the
pose which can be used to crop the reference maps, and therefore, limit the search area
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
39
==PAGE:56==
4.2. Estimation of the camera pose
of features. This cropping is essential for when the reference map is far larger than the
images, as it’s the case for both Mars 2020 and ONL.
Step 5
­ A lighter version of the DTM/DEM
In order to use the cropping feature, then the model of the surface is required. However,
the procedure for estimating the intercepting point between the optical axes of the camera
and terrain is not as efficient, as it makes a line which cuts through the terrain model
and then estimates the nearest surface point to this line, due to the number of points.
Therefore, it’s a good idea to make the map a less detailed, as it should only give a rough
idea of where to cut from.
Step 6
­ Estimation of pose function The pose estimation function is built on top of 4
main functions; cropping, matching, transformation between 2D image coordinates to 3D
world coordinates, and triangulation. However, as mentioned, then the optional features
can definitely speed things up, which therefore means that some choices are to be made.
If the features and a position estimation are added to the input of the function, then the
cropping process will be done on the features only, otherwise the cropping will be done
on the orthophoto. It should however be noted that the cropping is optional, but highly
recommend to use, and if it isn’t wanted, then an empty position is to be inserted instead
which will disable this function. After the cropping has been made, the features of the
cropped orthophoto is used together with the image from the test series in the function
called normalMatches. This function handles the feature matching and prefiltering by
estimating the fundamental matrix (not used, other than M­estimator Sample Consensus
(MSAC)). After matching of the features, then there should be several matches, as the
bare minimum is 4 in order to solve the P3P problem. However, to get better estimates,
then this should be higher. For Harris features, then 8 matched points can be used (as
trials has shown that this works good enough) and otherwise 15 points (to be on the safe
side). In order to use these points, then the corresponding points in 3D are found by using
the reference image which are bonded to a grid. However, the points in the test images
needs to be undistort from the lens distortions using the calibration details and the built­in
function undistortPoints to only undistort points of interest. The 2D image coordinates to
3D world coordinates transformation is done by using the built­in intrinsicToWorld, which
converts the 2D image point into a 2D world point (x, y). The last element/dimension
of the world point, the height, is found by using the DTM/DEM, where the point can be
interpolated. Lastly, the 3D points are used in the built­in function estworldpose to estimate
the world pose relative to the reference frame from the reference map. This function is
used in combination with variant of RANSAC(MSAC) as well to reduce the number of
outliers or points which has too much noise. This summarized in Figure 4.4, where the
different steps are shown.
autoCrop
­ functions
This function is made in different variants, one which is used solely for the Mars 2020 ref­
erence maps, and two for the ONL. The one used for the Mars 2020 case implements the
transformation between the epirectangular projection and MCMF, while the two tailored
for ONL, uses the existing reference frame. There are two version for the ONL case, one
for the reference image, and one for the features, which was made after Mars 2020 case.
normalMatches
­ function
This matching function uses the input of what kind of features to use and detect and extract
those features from the test image. This is followed by the same process of the orthophoto
