(optionally cropped), if the features haven’t been found beforehand. Then the features are
matched and sorted by strength of match. This leads to an additional optional setting, as
the strength of match can be used to limit the amount of matches. This setting should
only be used when there are too many matches where the pose estimation is too slow, as
it will greatly impact the result when there are very few matches. In order to reduce the
amount of false matches, then a RANSAC/MSAC can be done, as it’s better for removing
outliers, than just remove matches blindly based on the strength. The RANSAC/MSAC is
done by estimating the fundamental matrix, which can be used to fit the epipolar geometry
between the reference orthophoto and the test image, even though that this matrix isn’t
used. This is possible due to them being similar to stereo pairs, as they are viewing the
same scene from different views. This should however only be done when there are plenty
of matches, which is set to greater or equal to 15 matches. The reason for choosing the
fundamental matrix, is that it’s used for cases where the camera systems isn’t calibrated,
with the downside of requiring more matches than the essential matrix. An example of the
feature matching, can be seen in Figure 4.5, where the feature points are clearly found
and matches.
Step 7
­ Correct the reference coordinates for skewness, in order for the comparison of
accuracy to be better representative
This step is only relevant for the ONL case, as this was an error in the making of the
DTM. In order to do this, then the correction is done by calculating the corrections by
using the starting point of the corrections to subtract from the coordinates from the test
series images, and multiplying the correction equations which is shown in Equation 4.2.
Ideally, this shouldn’t be needed, but shows that the process of generating a DTM isn’t
just a walk in a park.

4.2.5
Differences between the Mars 2020 and the standard method, as
mentioned
The main adaption of the pose estimation algorithm is in the form of feature types and the
reference frame at which the reference points are given in.
Reference frame
difference
Mars 2020 uses both the epirectangular reference frame for the maps and the reference
coordinates for each image is given in the MCMF reference system. On the other hand,
then the test setup doesn’t use any standard reference frame, as it’s based on the robotic
cartesian reference frame. As the pose estimation algorithm is made to find the pose rel­
ative to the reference frame of the maps, then conversion is needed for Mars 2020, which
is done by using transformations between the epirectangular coordinates and latitude
and longitude, as the latitude and longitude is the universal conversion reference frame.
In order to convert from geodetic to geocentric latitudes, then this is done by using the
MATLAB command geocentricLatitude by defining the flattening of the Mars, the opposite
conversion is found from the command geodeticLatitudeFromGeocentric. MATLAB have
built­in functions to convert from ECEF or any other planetary body (for example MCMF)
with defined planet body to geodetic latitude, longitude and height/altimeter above the
ellipsoid. Therefore, these steps are necessary for having the autocropping functionality
and compare the results better. However, this may as well add some conversion errors
between the different reference frames, but these have been mitigated as much as pos­
sible. For the test setup, then reference frames were consistent, meaning that this error
source was eliminated, however, due to the introduction of the correction for the possi­
ble misalignment between the robotic arm and cameras, then this added another error
source.
Another difference is that the LCAM images are gaussian smoothed, where the stereo
images aren’t. This was needed for better performance, but the effect is not big, other
than the histogram of Figure 2.8b isn’t as quantized.
42
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
Feature
types
It was generally seen that Mars 2020 version of the algorithm worked quite good with
Harris features, while the ONL case worked best with SURF features. In addition to this,
then the matching process narrows the search window to the center of the LCAM images
in order to ignore the regions where the highest distortions appears. This difference is
rather small in terms of adaptions. However, the implication on the result can be quite
high, especially in the ONL case, as Harris features can’t be used for many of the images.
4.2.6
Changes made underway
At the start of making this algorithm, then it first tried to make the matching functions work­
ing. During this period of the project, then all the work done was with the Mars 2020 data.
The matching function was first done by making sure that features are matched between
the reference image and test image. After this was done, then two (actual three was in­
tended) functions were made to find the features and match them. The first which was im­
plemented was a coarse search which was using the entire Mars 2020 LCAM images and
reference image / SRM. In order to transition to a narrower search, then two terms were to
be met before proceeding to the narrower search; have three sequential times of having
60 % inside a region, and less than 250 pixel from the centre of the region. The narrower
search was done by firstly cropping the map to a smaller area of 1500 pixel × 1500 pixel,
and only extracting features in the centre of the Mars 2020 LCAM images(in order to keep
the distortions as low as possible. This may sound as a good idea with cropping by itself,
however one big draw back is that it estimates by itself where to crop based on previous
estimates, which means that it’s important that the coarse matches works great otherwise
it will maybe crop the SRM at another location which will end up resulting in zero matches.
Therefore, this was actually replaced with a simpler matching function that handled this
and introducing a new autocropping function which uses a position on the map to crop
from, as the position will most likely be estimated with help from an onboard IMUs in real
missions with some errors/noise. Another bigger change made underway was to split the
algorithm into two parts, one which is the script usage and one with the function where
the different processes are done. This resulted in a more clean code which can be un­
derstood far easier, however the earlier versions were intended to be quicker for debug
of the general method without the need of calling a function all the time.
4.2.7
Comparison between two different pose estimation methods
Pose estimation can be done by following different techniques. One way would be to
find features which are found in both the reference and test image. This is a traditional
feature matching based on the feature descriptor. The other method is about finding
the correlation between a feature and the reference image and from there extract the
where the feature is found in the reference image. This method will tend to have some
sort of feature detection and then matching the surrounding area of each feature onto
the reference image. In the beginning of this thesis, then, it was quickly decided to use
the feature matching based on the feature descriptor due to some experience working
with this. However, to compare these two methods, then a short comparison is shown
in Table 4.1, which is based on understanding from the underlaying theory or by seeing
examples.
From this short comparison, then, there are both pro and cons for selecting any of the
two main methods. When comparing to Mars 2020, then it makes sense to use the corre­
lation, as the computation resources of using feature descriptors may not be optimal for
onboard processing. On the other hand, if resources allows for this, then some benefits of
robustness to changes might be better, but this is where Mars 2020 made use of different
lightning conditions which were simulated, and the scaling of the reference image was

4.3
DTM and Orthophoto generation from ONL
The DTM and orthophoto generation is built on top of the general methods of finding 3D
points by triangulation, forming a terrain model by forming a surface from the 3D points,
and finally reprojecting 3D points on the terrain onto the image, to extract the image pixels.
Compared to the procedure for Mars 2020 map generation, then the general method of
making individual maps was used, where the overlaps are handled with a similar weighting
model. This is described further in subsection D.2.2. However, it was more difficult to look
up their specific method other than what has been written in [37]. Normally, this method
is not the best when there are overhangs over high points, which shadows other surface
features, however, as the terrain is mainly flat, then the procedure should be good to use.
The overhangs and shadowing features is normally handled by using different angles
of view, however that require a significant change to the individual map formation, as
the developed algorithm will make a smoother surface, and therefore remove/blur these
features. Another path would be to use a series of feature points, that is otherwise used
to triangulate these points, that would require a database of all the features used for
the triangulations which could then be used instead of doing it for a bigger map. The
disadvantages to this method, is that all features must be found and extracted, which could
mean that the memory needed would be higher than if a bigger map is used. Likewise,
the features must be located in terms of the overall reference frame, as positions relative
to a previous camera isn’t useful.
4.3.1
Requirements for maps
Due to the importance of the quality of the DTM and orthophoto, and being the key to a
well functioning estimation of the pose, then there are several requirements for such a
map. These requirements are shown below:
• Spatial resolution should be less or equal to 0.5mm, aiming at 0.15mm for a com­
promise between computing power and good enough amount of details
• Sizeable terrain model to fit the needed test series of additional images
44
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
• No abrupt shifts in elevations
• Noise shall be reduced, but finer details shall not be blurred too much
• Represent the terrain as seen on the orthophoto
• No overhangs or shadowing objects
For the orthophoto, then the requirements for this project:
• Spatial resolution should be less or equal to 0.5mm, aiming at 0.15mm for a com­
promise between computing power and good enough amount of details
• Sizeable terrain model to fit the needed test series of additional images
• No abrupt shifts in the pixels
• Noise shall be reduced if present, but finer details shall not be blurred too much
• Represent the orthophoto like it’s seen on the terrain
The reasoning behind the spatial resolution is that the resolution of the normal images are
around 1.3 mm/pixel, which therefore means that the spatial resolution should match this,
however some margin should be added, so 0.15 mm seems to be a good compromise. In
addition, then the covered area of the DTM and orthophoto should be as big as possible in
order to fit the test series images. Otherwise, there will be either partly covered or even no
coverage for specific positions. There shouldn’t be any abrupt shifts in either elevations
or pixels values, such as discontinuities. These abrupt shifts or discontinuities will typical
appear at overlapping regions where individual maps are combined, or when there are
errors in the estimated 3D points during the triangulation process. And of course, noise
shall be reduced as much as possible without losing the details, such as getting smooth
terrain models. For the DTM, then there is also the requirement regarding having no
overhangs or shadowing objects, as that will disturb the used method. This could for
instance be the astronaut figure standing on a hill in the test setup, as it will be almost
impossible to catch how it’s view from different angles when the shape is rather complex.
This was solved by not using this area specially, however, this figure was used to get a
visual reference by eye. And lastly, then the orthophoto and DTM shall match each other.
4.3.2
Overview of the method used and improvements made underway
The developed method is divided into several steps, which are shown in Figure 4.6. The
main datainput are the stereo pairs with the coordinates of the robotic arm, along with
calibration data such as the stereo camera calibration. The stereo pairs including coordi­
nates is initially loaded into two structures; the images (with settings) and a structure only
with the coordinates. Then the feature matches are found in the stereo pairs and triangu­
lated such that 3D points are found. These triangulated points are then used to make a
smaller DTM by using the Delaunay interpolation in MATLAB (griddata). This DTM now
corresponds to the 3D surface, where every point can be projected into the camera frame
to extract the pixel values. After this was done, some additional checks were done, where
the correction for the skewness of the reference frame was evaluated as well. These in­
dividual maps were then collected into a single larger map by using weighted averages
to interpolate the surface. After this step, then the final map can be reformatted into the
method that MATLAB uses to note down the reference grid. Lastly, features can be de­
tected and extracted from the map, as this will improve the speed of the pose estimation.
This algorithm has experienced a lot of changes; different number of neighbours for the
weighted average, different ways of making the 3D surface from the triangulated points,
other methods of assigning the pixel values, and finally the spatial resolution. One of the
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
45
==PAGE:62==
4.3. DTM and Orthophoto generation from ONL
most important changes made, was to use the variable type single instead of double, to
reduce the memory load, as these maps are based on a float numbers. Images were
also made sure to be 8­bit unsigned integers. In order to reduce the memory load, then
the algorithm was developed by serial processes only, as parallel processing here would
result in heavier load on the memory of the computer.

Step 2 ­ Generate individual maps (DTM+Orthophoto)
Step two in the generation of the final DTM and orthophoto is creation of the individual
maps. This step is one of the more complicated steps, as there are different tuning vari­
ables. An overview of the method is available in Figure 4.8, where the different parts can
be seen.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
47
==PAGE:64==
4.3. DTM and Orthophoto generation from ONL
Figure 4.8: Overview of how individual maps are generated, notation follows Figure 4.2
Firstly, the stereo pair images are loaded with the additional coordinates. Then it’s as­
sumed that the camera has been calibrated with the MATLAB stereo calibration tool, and
saved into a file. Then the actual map making can be done, which consist of two functions;
triangulation and actual map creation from the triangulated points. This is done for every
stereo pairs, and if something doesn’t look correct (by visual inspection of the DTM and
orthophoto), then it’s redone. After the individual maps has been created, then they can
be saved into a structure named Mapping. This structure contains the following fields:
• X ­ x­coordinates for each ’pixel’ in the robotic reference frame
• Y ­ y­coordinates for each ’pixel’ in the robotic reference frame
• Z ­ z­coordinates for each ’pixel’ in the robotic reference frame
• P_Left ­ 3D point projected into the left image to get pixel value
• P_Right ­ 3D point projected into the right image to get pixel value
• PLR ­ An array of where pixels have not been reprojected from 3D to 2D
• DN ­ The distance to nearest triangulated point
• wP ­ The triangulated points in the image in local reference frame which have an offset from
the robotic reference frame
• threshold ­ The threshold used
Triangulate given stereo pairs
function:
This function do the triangulation using the two stereo images. This means that the images
are the input with the calibration data as well. Two other inputs to the function are added
as well, with the ability to use gaussian smoothing of the images before triangulation
and reprojection error threshold. When this function was used to generate the current
48
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
individual maps, then the gaussian smoothing was set to zero, while the error threshold
was set to 30 pixels, which is rather high. In this function, then the first thing to do is
to convert to gray scale images, followed by the optional gauss smoothing. After this
conversion, then the images were undistorted by using the calibration data. Normally
this isn’t efficient, as it should ideally only be done on individual points of interests POIs,
however this was done in order to remove it at the beginning. Then the actual triangulation
is done, which is initially done by detecting surface features. This is done by choosing
what kind of feature type and relevant settings. It was chosen that SURF features was the
best a yielding the best amount of matches while keeping fast enough, which was further
tuned with the built­in setting of MetricThreshold. This was done with a setting of 1500
compared to the default 1000, which then yielded fewer features, as the images are in
high resolution. Optimally, then there should be as many features as possible, however,
the terrain is challenging for this purpose as it’s mostly the same surface, and therefore
it takes longer time, when comparing features which looks the same. In order to speed
up the process, after the features has been detected, then features which are naturally
not seen in both camera views are removed, which is practical done by only including a
slightly bigger overlapping region where features are located. This was seen to be around
1200 pixels from the edges away from the other camera view horizontal, and therefore a
common region composes of 4800 pixels from each image. After this optimization, then
the feature descriptor was extracted, and the feature sets for the stereo pair is matched.
After this step, then one can choose to remove weak matches (strength is part of the
built­in matching function), or simply just use all. This was a remnant from the pose
estimation method, which has not been used here, but the ability still exist. In order to
reduce the amount of false matches, then an initial RANSAC/MSAC is done by using
the built­in function called estimateFundamentalMatrix, which estimates the fundamental
matrix, which can be used to detect which matches that fits the epipolar geometry of
the stereo cameras, and therefore detect those who doesn’t. After this RANSAC/MSAC,
a triangulation is done by using these matched features locations in the image and the
stereo parameters such as the relative pose between the two cameras in order to solve
the P3P problem. This can however result in the revelation of points which have higher
reprojection error than others and therefore most likely being outliers or with too much
noise. The reprojection error is simply just the error when reprojecting the 3D points onto
the 2D image reference frame. These reprojection errors are then used to filter out these
points by setting a threshold. It should be noted that the Z­components is relative to
the centre of the camera, resulting in near objects having smaller distances than others.
This is corrected when reformatting the final map, as this is also key information when
combining the individual maps.
Use triangulated points for creating maps
function:
The next function uses the triangulated points in addition to the images, calibration data
and coordinates. This function, essentially, uses the triangulated points to form a terrain
model with the associated orthophoto. Firstly, a threshold is used to define a maximum
distance to other triangulated points. This used to make the process more robust, as the
used threshold of 50 mm dictates that there should be at least one other triangulated point
in reach of that point. If this value is too high, then maybe some triangulated points which
are far from the other ones have been passed through the RANSAC/MSAC routine and
other filters from triangulation function mentioned above, which it shouldn’t have been.
A value, too low, may reduce too many points. Therefore, this threshold should be fit­
ting the data to give a good result, as more triangulated points normally results in better
detailed map, but maybe also introduce errors in form of noise. Furthermore, if some of
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
49
==PAGE:66==
4.3. DTM and Orthophoto generation from ONL
these points are not at the expected distance from the camera, then these should be re­
moved. This was indirectly implemented when seeing that triangulated points were being
located to be much closer to the lens than any other objects, or even behind the camera.
Therefore, this was limited by setting a minimum distance from the camera that should be
complied to, so no directly limitation with the distance interval, but just a simple boundary
towards the camera. After this preprocessing of the triangulated points, then a grid is
formed which spans the rectangle area from the minimum and maximum coordinates of
the triangulated points, in addition to initializing the DTM/z as NaN values. During this
step, then in the early development of this function, it was seen that some points also
appeared far from the others, which was later fixed with the filtration. This is still part of
the code, but not actively used. All of this leads up to the actual creation of the map, which
was firstly done in one way, which was then changed to using a built­in function.
The first method
­ knnsearch
The first method used a weighted average to find the DTM and orthophoto. This involved
choosing the number of neighbours to use, which is an important parameter, as it works
great in some cases, and in other very poorly. In order to demonstrate this, then Figure 4.9
show a case of making of a map, where the black dots are the triangulated points, and the
colours indicates the z­component. The colours indicate that the areas where there are
plenty of points are smoothly estimated with the z­components. However, areas where
there are less dense of points, have spots around the triangulated points. This is a result
of the weighting model when too many neighbours are included, since the weights of
nearest points gets diluted by the amount of points which may be clustered. Looking at
the worst case in the bottom right corner, then there is only 9 points in this area which are
approximately at the same height. If the number of neighbours is set to 50, then around
40 points located at the blue region will be used, which results in having the sum being
diluted by this number.
Figure 4.9: Initial method of interpolating to 3D surface, where dots represent the trian­
gulated points.
After the number of neighbours has been defined, then in order to make most used of
the computation power and keeping the memory requirement low (lower memory means
lower performance), then the processing was done in steps, by defining these steps. The
50
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
steps are defined by choosing only a part of the grid points, ngrid points, to search each
time. This will further reduce the memory allocation as the knnsearch of MATLAB defines
the output sizes as ngrid points × k, where k is the number of neighbours, however the
internal memory requires arrays of size ngrid points×ntriangulated points. Therefore, reducing
ngrid points will efficiently reduce the memory load, as it can be done in steps since the
number of triangulated points ntriangulated points remains the same. In the code, then some
optimization is done by initializing the variables, which preallocates the memory. Then
the neighbour search is done in these steps, which then notes which neighbouring points
that are closest and the distance to them. In addition, then the distance to the nearest
neighbours is noted as well, with the purpose of it being optionally used for weighting in
later steps. Then the weighting is formed by using the distances, where they are firstly
normalized with the closest distance, such that it will result in a weight of 1 for closest and
then decreasing. After this, then the weighed sum can then be done by normalizing the
weights for the neighbours to a sum of 1, which is part of the weighted average. And then
the normalized weights are then multiplied with the z­heights in order to get the weighted
average of the neighbour’s z­heights. This is shown in Equation 4.1
wi = min(d)
di
Wi =
wi
∑k
i=1 wi
Z =
k
∑
i=1
Wi zi
(4.1)
The final method
­ Delaunay triangulation
The other method is to use the built’in function like griddata, which uses the Delaunay tri­
angulation. The Delaunay triangulation is based on using the triangulated points to form
a 3D surface, where the benefits are that it can be used to interpolate these point into a
grid. MATLAB then have different methods of using this depending on the specific data.
MATLAB has an option to be continuous at different orders of derivative functions. The
linear model is a simple triangulation which is basically just lines/planes between points,
where the continuity is for being at the same point location, and therefore discontinues
can happen when looking at the slopes. On the other hand, then the cubic is continu­
ous along the second derivative, meaning that a more smooth terrain will be formed. A
compromise or combination between the two methods is the natural neighbours, where
it’s continuous along the first derivative function except at the triangulated points, which
can not be guaranteed. An example of the map generated can be seen in Figure 4.10a
The distance to the neighbouring points can then found from with the knnsearch function,
which is shown in Figure 4.10c, where the red dots indicate the triangulated points, and
colouring is based on the distance. The scale can also be limited to 40 mm, which re­
sults in enhancing the smaller details, where a cell structure appears (Figure 4.10d). This
hasn’t been used as additional weight since it can have too much weight on some pixels,
which is at the triangulate point.
Getting the pixel values
To generate the orthophoto, then the definition an orthophoto
is used. An orthophoto is defined to be an image which has pixels corresponding to
specific coordinates as seen from above. This means that if these coordinates can be
projected into the image reference system, then the closest pixel value can be extracted
or even interpolated. This is done in both image views by first setting the camera view left)
to be pointing downward, and then transforming the 3D coordinate into the corresponding

2D image point, ending with extracting the pixel value. Here, it’s simple usage of the
camera model, where the camera parameters are taken into considerations. For the right
image, then the relative pose to camera is used, as the triangulated points are found
relative to the first camera. At the end of this function, then the offsets are added to the
coordinates, such that the centre of the left camera is set to the pose of the robotic arm.
This however assumes that the robotic arm and camera is well aligned, as any angles
will result in a skew. This skewness was actually detected, which is why a correction has
been added to reduce this effect. Furthermore, as the triangulation may not cover the
entire grid, as the Delaunay triangulation doesn’t extrapolate from the area span by the
points, which resulted in were set to NaN. The corresponding image from the 3D surface
in Figure 4.10a, can be seen in Figure 4.10b, here it should be noted that the image isn’t
rectangular, which is due to the Delaunay triangulation, which doesn’t extrapolate from the
area span by the points. Here it can likewise be seen that it’s per definition an orthophoto,
since the image pixels corresponds to the pixels of 3D points as seen from above.
Correction loop
As it’s mentioned, then some errors may not be caught automatically, which is why a
manual check is done for every map. This is practical done by visualizing the result from
the last function(Use triangulated points for creating maps) and accept this and continue
or simply stop and try redoing the triangulation. For cases which were severe, and before
some of the filtration, then the points were manually deleted, and the individual map is
52
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies

Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
redone.
4.3.4
Correct for skewness of the reference frame
As mentioned, then, due to the usage of the robotic reference being applied to the left
camera centre, then it’s very important to keep everything aligned, especially since the
camera is offset from the correct centre of the robotic arm. This is skewness applies
when keeping track of a single point in an image and extract the 3D coordinate from
the individual maps. This should ideally be at the exact same location, however if there
is any misalignment, then some skewness is introduced. This result in in some severe
misalignments, as in Figure 4.11, where the three similar sizes craters at x = −200 mm
and y = 2650 mm are not aligned as they are in reality.
Figure 4.11: Orthophoto, which have errors due to skewness of the reference frame.
Made with the settings of T=700, k=150 with the knn method
In order to correct for this, then the skewness is first inspected by noting down the exact
coordinate that this appears in different maps. This can be done in MATLAB by simply
using getpts, which lets the user select a point and extract the coordinates at that point,
which is illustrated in Figure 4.12a. This is done for a series of the individual maps that
covers a specific region, which results in the coordinates seen in the figure as dots. It can
be seen from Figure 4.12b, that there is a tendency or correlation between the rail/y and x
positions of the robotic arm and the deviations. Therefore, it can be seen that this isn’t just
noise, which means that it can be corrected to some degree. Ideally, it should be explored
in details in order to correct before this step, with angles etc., however a workaround was
to add a correction based on the position along the rail/y and x to the coordinates. This
resulted in the following correction terms, the terms were found by trial and error, and the
reasoning behind the sine and cosine terms was due to an initial idea of it being some
kind of misalignment. ∆Rail is found by using the between the first position of the robotic

4.3.5
Generate a single map covering the individual maps
To generate a single larger map from the smaller individual maps, then the script Map­
forming is used. The overview can be seen in Figure 4.13.
Step 1
­ Loading the data
As the first step, then the individual maps are loaded into MATLAB, including the correc­
tions. If the additional/optional weighting by the distance to a triangulated point is to be
used, then this used, and if it hasn’t been found (for older versions), then this can be done.
Step 2
­ Fadding/edge weights
The next step is to make the overlapping process of the individual maps better. This is
done when introducing a weighting model, such that the edge of the maps gets to have
zero weight, further from the edge increases to a weight of 1. There exist different weight­
ing models, which all depends on the data. The weighting is done in the function called

BorderWeight_Irregular, which uses the constant T(weight parameter that is tunable), a
variable to denote where there is data in for example height map (valid data point in the
height matrix), and then the method. To add the weights, then the MATLAB command
bwdist is used to find the distance to the edges, which is noted down as NaN values
which is further padded to a template (extracted NaN values is noted as 1, otherwise 0).
Then the distance can be directly used in the weighting model. The first way of adding
the weights was rather time­consuming, as the weights was done by diluting the edges
gradually, and adding the corresponding weights. This however was too simple, as irreg­
ular shapes which aren’t rectangular would have weights that isn’t corresponding to the
correct distance to the edge, and therefore using bwdist, where the edges are noted as
1, then the correct distance is found.
Distance
weighting model
The simplest weighting model is as simple as only including the distance to edge of the
maps. The downside to this method is that it can’t be used as it is, since it isn’t normalized
or limited to be in the range of zero to one.
Exponential
weighting model
Another weighting method would be a form of exponential function. This could for instance
be as Equation 4.3.
w = 1 −e−n
T
(4.3)
This weighting model, w, is normalized to the range of 0 to 1, when the distance n to
the edge is zero or positive, and the constant T is positive and non­zero. This model is
similar to many models involving electronics, where T is used as time constant, where the
expression will reach 63.2 % of the final value which is here 1. Here, T, is the distance
from the edges at which the weight becomes 0.632, where if n = T, then this is the weight
at this distance. A visual rule of thumb, then by multiplying with 5 will approximately reach
1(99.33 %). The exponential weighting model can be seen in Figure 4.14, with T ≃100.
The downside to this model, is that it’s rather difficult to limit a significant part of the edge
and have a steep curve, which normally would result in having a weight in the centerpoint

in a map being lower than 1 when a significant part of the edge should have lower weights.
Therefore, during the later part of the project, another weighting model was implemented.

S­curve
weighting model
The S­curve model have several features that the exponential function doesn’t have. It
uses a higher order polynomial to make a smooth transition from zero to one. The im­
plemented one here is formed by Ken Perlin in 2002[38], which made the improvements
of having the derivatives at x = 0 and x = 1 being zeros, and at the same time being
continuous, which is shown in section C.1. The importance of this model, is that it’s lim­
ited, as the function will increase to infinity as the distance to the edges increases, which
means that the centre of the maps will get very large weights. The general model can be
seen in Equation 4.4, where f is the output which is used as weight and x is simply the
normalized parameter.
f = 0
x < 0
f = 6 x5 −15 x4 + 10 x3
1 >= x >= 0
f = 1
x > 1
(4.4)
As Equation 4.4 can not be used to input the distance into x, then x can be expanded to
be the fraction n

Here it can be seen that the distance n is only used from 0 to T, and to denote that’s the
weight, then f is replaced by w. Furthermore, a feature of Equation 4.5, is that when the
fraction is equal to 1
2, then w = 1
2. This model can also be used by multiplying with itself
to form a higher order function, this will however push the halfway point to the right and
make the weight of the edges be closer to zero. This will still comply with the derivatives
being zero and still continuous, but it hasn’t been used. This however can not be done the
other way (nth root), as the derivatives may not be zero any more, and there will be some
discontinuities. The weighting model can be seen in Figure 4.14. An example of use can
be seen in Figure 4.15a, where four different signals with some noises are shown, where

Step 3
­ Initialization ­ Individual maps
The third step includes the initialization of the variables which are used to have the indi­
vidual map data. This is done, so single variables can have all maps details, for example
the x­coordinates.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
57
==PAGE:74==
4.3. DTM and Orthophoto generation from ONL
Step 4
­ Initialization ­ Final map
This step is about forming the grid of the final map, which is done in the steps of 0.15 mm.
During this step, then the individual map data that has been added in the previous step is
filtered such that any points, which aren’t in the final map grid, are removed. This points
which are removed, is likewise removed from other variables, like weight etc. In addition
to these things, then the reshaping into vectors is done to work in this way for simplification
of the process of finding neighbours.
Step 5
­ The number of neighbours
The next step involves setting the number of neighbours, k, to use of combining the differ­
ent individual maps. This is a highly tunable parameter, as more neighbours will result in
a smoother map, but details may not appear as good. A good result was seen at k = 40,
which produced a good map, but this wasn’t studied in greater details to find the most
optimal value, as it can be difficult to make a direct comparison for what is best when a
perfect and true model doesn’t exist.
Step 5
­ Division into subparts
The next coming task is splitted into several parts, depending on the number of grid points
divided by 20. This means that more points/larger map splits the task into more pieces.
The number 20 is a random found constant which seems to work alright on the specific
computer, as other system may work better with less demanding tasks which are splitted
more, than others.
Step 6
­ The actual map forming part ­ Initial
The way that the subparts are divided is actually quite good, as it contains a column of
the grid data at a time. This is used to define an overlap, which in the project was set
to ±3 mm. This is used to ignore points from the individual maps which are far from this
region. If the weighting with the distance to nearest triangulated point, then this can be
used here as well.
Step 7
­ Forming the weights
After the points from the individual maps are selected, then the knnsearch function is
once again used to extract the nearest k neighbours for each grid point. When knowing
the nearest neighbours and the distances, then the complete weighting model can be
formed by firstly normalizing the distances to the nearest, such that a neighbour which are
the double distance from the grid point than the nearest neighbour will have a normalized
distance of 2. Then the weight is formed by finding the inverse, which results in the nearest
neighbour getting a weight of 1 and decreasing with distance. An assumption here is that
the distance is larger than 0, otherwise a division with zero would occur. To combine these
weights with the edge fadding weights, then they can be multiplied with each other. The
weighting model, which was found not to be good enough for the individual maps are here
used, has been chosen here, as the individual map provides many points each in their
own grids, which reduces the dilution effect from points further away.
Step 8
­ The actual map forming part
To form the map, then the weights are normalized by their sum, which is done for each grid
point. To make the weighted average, then the weights are multiplied with the neighbours
heights, which results in a weighted average of the heights. To extract the pixel for that
point, then different methods exist. Either one can take the weighted average or just
average. However, that poses a risk of blurring details if the individual maps are not
58
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
exactly aligned. Another method is to use the point in the individual maps with the highest
weight. This is the used method, as it ensures that there is no added blurring effect of the
final orthophoto. This is then done for the rest of the grid points.
Step 9
­ Reshaping back into matrices and saving the map
In this step, then the grid data is reshaped back into the original matrix forms. And at the
end of this step, then the map is saved for the next part.
4.3.6
Reformat the map to fit within the pose estimation algorithm
This part is rather simple, as it just converts the map from the previous part into a map
format that MATLAB operate with by loading the saved map. This involves referring the
height map and orthophoto to a specific reference frame. This is done using maprefpost­
ings and maprefcells. A downside for choosing the grid point format previously, is that
the images are normally showed with the rows(y­direction) starting from the top and in­
creasing downwards, which means that the image will be flipped upside­down. In order
to fix this, then the height map and orthophoto is flipped, and the corresponding reference
is changed accordingly. One note, however, is that the x−and y−coordinates are con­
verted to m from mm. In addition to the found height map Z, which is renamed to ZDTM,
then a variable called Height is also formed by defining the lowest point in the terrain
(furthest from camera) as zero, and then highest being the new maximum point. These
map references, ZDTM, Height and orthophoto is then saved to be the final output.
4.3.7
Detect and extract features from the map
As mentioned, then an optional step can be done, which speeds up the process of pose
estimation. This involves detecting and extracting features from the Orthophoto. This is
rather straight forward, as the Orthophoto is converted to greyscale, and then it’s just a
matter of choosing which features to detect. In this project then a several feature types
and specific settings are chosen, some have been used, other not. The used features
are shown in Table 4.2. These features can then be saved to the same folder as the DTM
and orthophoto.
Feature type

per octave (NSL)=4, MT850

4.3.8
General optimization, which is essential to make the code run
As the map generation is probably the most demanding task regarding computing power,
then optimizations are important to reduce the load. This was done underway by splitting
the parts into several smaller parts which reduced the memory load, which in turns make
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
59
==PAGE:76==
4.4. Detection of hazards
the computation quicker as the usage of slower SWAP memory is reduced. Furthermore,
a greater optimization was to use the variable type single instead of double, since the pre­
cision and size of variables values of the project fits very well to this size. This reduced
essentially the half of the memory load since the double type uses 4 bytes, and where sin­
gle only uses 8 bytes. And this is likewise also done for the images when possible, where
the conversion to uint8 was used, as the original images are already in this datatype.
4.4
Detection of hazards
Due to the high relevance between terrain relative navigation and hazards avoidance,
then detection of these hazards is important as well.
4.4.1
Goal for this project
The goal of this part of the thesis is to study if hazards can be detected by using the
features. As it has been explained, then Mars 2020 had an onboard safe target map,
where hazards and dangerous terrain was premapped. This meant that eventual new
or smaller hazards were not being taken into consideration at the time of landing other
than distribution models of smaller rocks. However, if this system was able to detect
these ’live’ for the descent images, then the hazard avoidance system will likely be better
at estimating real dangers that can only be seen closer to the surface. So this part will
mainly just be about study the step in the pose estimation where the features are found
in the test/descent image, and if these features can be used to extract some of the larger
hazards overall. Furthermore, a slope map will also be found including the direction of the
slopes, which will now be discussed on how to extract this information from a DEM/DTM.
The general discussion of using these features is in the discussion part of the Mars 2020
hazards section.
4.4.2
Description of how to find the slopes, including the directions
As mentioned subsubsection 3.3.1, then a DEM/DTM can be viewed similar to an image,
where the slopes can easily be identified by using gradient filters, specially Equation 3.26
and 3.27 which can be convoluted with the height map. The benefit of using this method
is that it’s fast, and additional filters can be added that will smooth the surface if needed.
The slopes along the two axes can then be used to find the magnitude of the slope, which
once again can be converted into an angle and then the direction relative to the axes. The
downside is that it’s highly limited by the resolution of the map, and using Equation 3.26
and 3.27 will only look at a total of four neighbouring points, which means that noise can
be a problem. One way to mitigate this, is to use another filter to average the slopes,
which likely will reduce the details, unfortunately. The best examples can be seen in
subsubsection 6.1.2, where the DEM from Mars 2020 are used to detect the slopes

The following helper functions, which hasn’t been explained yet, will now be explained
briefly in how they generally work. These are separated into three main use cases, either
general, Mars 2020 or only used in the test case. There are plenty of functions and scripts
which are included in the zip file attached to the report, but some of these are not as well
documented with comments.
4.5.1
General

simple figure saving, where the output can be changed in size, and if the output is as a
pdf, then vectorize it if wanted. getImagecenter, estimates where the directional vector
intersects the 3D surface

4.6
Getting the necessary images and maps
4.6.1
Source
The images that have been used throughout the project can be separated into two main
categories; previous mission and experimental tests. The source of the experimental tests
is quite straightforward, as they have been captured in the ONL shown as the frontpage
image as well. The sources for the images of previous mission comes from different
websites. The Mars 2020 images from onboard cameras can be found in the PDS portal,
where the camera parameters are added as header to the .IMG files.[5] The reference
image(orthophoto) and elevation map can be downloaded either through NASA TREK, or
by finding them in the PDS archieve [8][9]. These have the format of a geotiff, where the
reference frame is included as well in that file.
4.6.2
Reading the images and maps
Since there are many different formats, then this part will be separated into the different
cases.
Mars 2020
As mentioned above, then the output from the onboard cameras of Mars 2020 is available
as .IMG format. There exist a thumbnail or preview files as .PNG format, however they
are heavily quantized. To download the images, then they can either be downloaded one
by one, or be downloaded with a script. In this project it was just downloaded manually
as it was quicker to do. There exist an ’inventory’ file of the different files which can be
used to make a script.
The file name of the .IMG files is made by the following fields:
ELM_0000_0666952900_100FDR_N0000067LVS_04000_0000LUJ01.IMG
By following the file name structuring from [7, p. 193­206], then the following can be
extracted from the file name:
• EL: EDL Lander Vision System (LCAM)
• M: Greyscale/monochrome image
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
61
==PAGE:78==
4.6. Getting the necessary images and maps
• 0000: Sol 0
• LU: Lossless and uncompressed
• J: JPL
To read this fileformat, then the header can be extracted from the file data. The header
consist of two parts, where it’s more or less the same content (same values for those
that are used in this project), but formatted slightly different. An example of some of
the fields can be seen in section D.3.
But the first part is the actual label, which is
nicely formatted to be readable and can be extracted as a struct in MATLAB by using
the developed function called IMG2imdatabase, which transform the .IMG format into
an image and includes the different header details. of.From the header, then the fol­
lowing can be extracted: There are 1037 records (FILE_RECORDS), each of size 1024
(RECORD_BYTES), and the 8 first records contains the label (LABEL_RECORDS). The
image itself starts from the 14 record to the end (ÎMAGE), where the image header is
from record 9 until the image data (ÎMAGE_HEADER). The camera model can be seen un­
der /*CAMERA_MODELDATAELEMENTS*/ where the CAHVORE model has been used.
The parameters can be used to ’decode’ the image by reading it as unsigned integer of 8
bit. This is done in matlab using the fread function, where the data is extracted as 1024
by 1037 data, where the header can be removed. 1
The complete header of the same image can be seen in D.1
For the DEM and the SRM or reference/orthophoto image, then the format available was
geotiff. This format has the benefit of being defined to a specific reference grid, where the
distance between each pixel is fixed. Normally, then the geotiff can be in either rectangular
grid or geographic grid, meaning either in a grid defined by angles like latitude/longitude or
metric like meters. This is then accompanied by more parameters of the projection. The
geotiff image can then consist of either data/measurement corresponding to each point
in the grid, the height of the terrain, or image pixels. The DEM and reference images are
available as the same ones as those which are onboard Mars 2020 and therefore used by
the LVS. The DEM are also available at higher resolutions for certain parts, which has been
used to map hazards prior to usage.To read the geotiff images/data into MATLAB, then
the built­in function called readgeoraster, can be used, where commands like mapcrop,
mapresize and mapinterp can be used to crop, resize and interpolate the image/data
respectively. By working in this kind of environment in MATLAB, then the reference grid
and image/data can be updated efficiently, while the image data can then be used as a
normal image with filters, feature matching etc.
4.6.3
Apollo
While Mars 2020 have the best data for the purpose of a LVS, then other missions has
imaged or mapped other planetary bodies as well. This involves the Apollo era, where
numerous images from orbit were taken of the surface of the Moon. These images can
potentially be used in the same developed algorithm, where it’s not descending images,
but just images from orbit. However, the key requirements for running the algorithm is the
DEM, and reference image/orthophoto. This means that the surface of interest must be
mapped by the elevation of the terrain, and a reference image must be generated prior
to using this kind of pose estimation. The Apollo missions didn’t have the possibility to
generate this kind of data, which means that other missions must be used. Due to lack
1The file format was elaborated from email correspondence with David Mayer from Astrogeology Science
Center ­ USGS
62
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 4. Implementation and synthesis
of the technology that exist today, then a complete map of the Moon with high resolu­
tion data was first possible with missions like LRO, where complete DEM[12] has been
formed. However, the availability of reference images which comply with the requirement
of being an orthophoto where the reference system is well known, is very sparse on the
internet when looking for high resolution images. This can be quite surprising compared
to Mars, where Jezero crater has been mapped in far greater details. However, as it can
be seen from the case of Mars, then the high detail images and maps were generated for
Jezero crater only due to the mission, as this can be quite cumbersome task of forming a
complete map and reference images, which is combined correctly. There could also be
other reasons to why Mars is maybe more available, like the interest of Mars for manned
missions and the big questions on the history of Mars regarding previous oceans and
lifeforms. Mars is far more difficult to land at, due to both the influence of the weak at­
mosphere, stronger gravity and the distance from Earth compared to Mars. Thus, that
doesn’t imply that the task is even easier on the Moon, as simple pose estimation still
remains unavailable from high accuracy sources like GNSS that SpaceX uses to land
rockets on Earth. Therefore, if It was possible, then a dataset from the Moon will provide
additional insights in the effects on the algorithm that the different terrain features have.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies

==PAGE:80==
5
Test setup
5.1
Key requirements for test series
The key requirements that is required to have a good pose estimation based on the sim­
ulated surface, then the following is needed:
• Stereo setup remains constant throughout the entire test series from mapping to the
’descent’ images
• Cameras have known intrinsic and distortions for each test series
• Surface terrain remain constant from mapping onto the ’descent’ images.
• Coordinates should be noted down for comparison
• The reference frame should remain the same from the one used by the mapping
procedure, this includes the program which keeps track of the robotic arm at the
controls.
In order to estimate the pose, then the input of the reference map and image requires
additional things:
• Stereo setup is calibrated in regard to the relative pose to each other
• Same lighting conditions for the mapping procedure

5.3
Stereo Vision use cases for space missions
At the start of the project, then an idea to improve the landing accuracy with basically no
requirement of previous mapping would be to implement a stereo vision as the landing
camera. This however have some advantages and disadvantages as what will now be
discussed. But to conclude for this, then stereo cameras onboard landers for the use case
of landing will not be realistic to use, as the baseline either have to be unrealistic high,
FOV too small, or the pixels of the sensor being too small.
5.3.1
The advantages of using stereo vision cameras during the landing
phase
Stereo vision cameras has the benefits of being able to perceive depth information. This
information will therefore allow the lander to ’see’ in 3D, and thus be able to get a relative
position with regard to some 3D points. The use case for this is the ability to tell if there are
obstacles straight ahead and their position relative to the cameras. Mars 2020, consist of
many different cameras, where the hazard avoidance for driving on the surface is done
with stereo vision, which therefore will provide this ability to the rover.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
65
==PAGE:82==
5.4. Discussion of using existing manmade objects for reference
5.3.2
The disadvantages of using stereo vision cameras during the
landing phase
The disadvantages are rather demotivating for using stereo vision for the landing phase.
The main disadvantages are based on the fundamental stereo vision model and how it
perceives depth, and the fact that using stereo vision alone will only provide a relative
pose estimation for the specific 3D terrain. As mentioned and shown in the theory (sub­
section 3.6.1), then in order to perceive depth, one can either increase the focal length
(decreasing the FOV) or increase the baseline. Increasing the focal length will generally
not be the best option, as the pose estimation that should be done require a good amount
of matches spread across a bigger area. Reducing this area will generally mean the that
uncertainties will get larger, as the points will be located near each other. This is just
like in the case of PnP. Therefore, increasing the focal length will not work. Increasing
the baseline will likewise also be quite bad, as the physical size of the rovers are much
smaller than what it’s required to have a stereo vision setup for this use. This can be seen
in Figure 3.9, where increasing the focal length increases the depth perception.
As it can be seen from Equation 3.42, then the disparity is as important. xr −xl denotes
the physical disparity on the sensor. This means that in order to increase Z, then there
is an actual way of doing it, with the downside of it being problematic by itself. By de­
creasing the physical disparity to the individual pixel size (disparity of 1 pixel), the only
way of decreasing it further is to make the pixels smaller, which means that more pixels
are needed to cover the same area. This may sound doable, but a significant disadvan­
tage occurs when the sensors elements are downscaled and more are introduced at the
same time. As it was shortly discussed in section 3.1, then increasing the pixels will lead
to a CCD sensor being slower and therefore the frame rate. CMOS on the other hand will
struggle with noise, as each pixel will contain amplifiers which will heat up and introduce
thermal noise. Furthermore, having higher amount of pixel will also slow down any image
processing, and decreasing the size, likewise decreases the signal and therefore longer
exposures are needed. Therefore, decreasing the physical disparity by decreasing the
pixel size is also not the way. However, for quick comparisons then doubling the focal
length or the baseline will double the depth that can be perceived for a specific disparity,
while decreasing the disparity will as well double the perceivable depth while the focal
length and baseline is kept constant.
5.3.3
Alternative use cases
Stereo vision can however also be used quite differently. A stereo vision setup doesn’t
require that the cameras are equal (that however would simplify things considerably), nor
that the images are taken at the same time. What this means is that a virtual stereo
camera can be made by taking two images either by two satellites or the same satellite
from different positions and then extract the depth information. This, however, require a
very accurate pose estimate of the satellites, as that determines the final accuracy of the
depth information.
5.4
Discussion of using existing manmade objects for
reference
In order to use manmade objects or other specific objects on the surface for exact refer­
ence points, then these object must be able to be clearly visible and distinguishable from
other objects. This means that the image scale must be able to define the properties of
the objects, which translates to for instance with the DEM and SRM of Mars 2020 with a
scale of 6 m/pixel being at least 3 pixels wide in either direction to differentiate edges and
the main body of the object relative to the surrounding surface. This means that it should
66
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 5. Test setup
be at least be 18 m following this condition, however that is still very sensitive to noise. It
should be noted that the scale corresponds to the scale of the LCAM at 3 km[1]. In order
to estimate the pose, then several of these object must be set up, which requires that sev­
eral large objects which doesn’t get weathered to look like the surrounding surface should
be brought to Mars, or existing larger rocks should be used. So this might not be relevant
from high altitudes, but rather at low altitudes to accurate landing on manmade landing
platforms when getting near a colony, which means that the landing target should still be
targetting near the base. For comparison, then SpaceX Landing Zones have a diame­
ter of 86 m(282ft[39]), which means that these landing pads does have the correct size
of more than 14 pixel. However, on Mars, dust may cover the pad, meaning that it may
not be as visible and distinguishable in the long term. So in other words, then structures
built in height might be a better alternative. However, imaging a Martian colony having
a cleaned landing pad and other landers in closer proximity that is very distinguishable,
would be very useful for landing at a specified landing pad.

67
==PAGE:84==
6
Discussion and presentation of results
In this part of the report, then the results from the work done will be presented and dis­
cussed, where the two cases will be compared in the end.
6.1
Mars 2020
6.1.1
Pose accuracy
The pose estimation for the Mars 2020 was done by using either SURF features (default
settings) or Harris features (Minimum quality for the LCAM and reference image was set to
respective 0.01 and 0.001). Other features were tried, as the coded is already prepared for
this, but the results were far from good, as SIFT for instance doesn’t return many (if any)
matches which may be because of the input images. This wasn’t fine­tuned other than to
get the time down to a manageable time (under 2 s). At first, then the two pose estimation
results will be presented separately and then compared afterwards. It should however
be noted that this doesn’t include the fish eye effect, neither the EKF which improved the
estimation accuracy significantly.
SURF based estimation
Overview
­ 3D figures
The estimated poses can be seen in Figure 6.1, where the left figure shows the region
that is cropped, which is shown to the right. The figure to the right, is the estimated poses
of the cameras where it’s quite visible that the estimation is not perfect. Figure 6.2a
shows the estimated and true trajectory (from the camera header), where Figure 6.2b are
the 2D plane in the x and y plane. Both of these figures shows how much that the pose
estimation deviates from the true trajectory. A thing to note from this figure is that the pose
estimation have only estimated poses in a part of the trajectory, which can be expected
as the resolution and details doesn’t work in the favour of the algorithm when the altitude
is too high. One could then skip the figures showing how much this actual is, but they are
presented as well.

In order to make more use of this, then the azimuth angle (angle from the x axis and to
the vector component in the x and y plane) and nadir angle (angle from the negative z
axis to the directional vector, by using the geometry with the angle from the z axis and the
directional vector which is subtracted from 180° to yield the nadir angle) can be introduced,
as it will aid in the understanding on where the points deviate. Figure 6.5a shows the
azimuth and nadir angles, where it can be seen that there are several differences. It should
be noted that this is not the spacecraft reference frame, meaning that the tumbling etc.
will not be very obvious from this. Here it can be seen that the azimuth angle stays almost
constant in the true pose, which makes sense since it travels in the same direction. The
nadir angle seems to increase in the start, which could be just the spacecraft adjusting.
Then the nadir angle decreases, which most likely can be explained by the braking force
of the parachute, which decreases the horizontal velocity as well. The difference between
the estimated and true angles in Figure 6.5b, then it can be seen that the pose estimation
works quite badly in this case, where the nadir angle should otherwise be estimated better.
The azimuth angle can deviate depending on the nadir angle (when nadir angles gets
closer to zero, then it can result in more deviations as any change might have bigger
impact).

Performance
of the pose algorithm
In order to see how well the pose estimation works, then the performance parameters
like the time and number of matched points. In general, lower time and higher number of
matched points or features is better. Figure 6.8a shows the time that it took to run the pose
estimation algorithm on the 87 LCAM images. It can be seen that the initial images takes
longer time, which can be caused by the mismatch between the scales, as the spacecraft
is far from the surface and therefore the pixel scale is far from the reference image pixel
scale, or it can be caused by an inrun time of MATLAB, where it gets more efficient after
a couple of repetitions. This continues until reaching around image 24. When looking at
Figure 6.8b, then it can be seen that there are many matches from image 20 until image
40, but not as many as the case of LVS, where it was up to 150, which was reduced to
around 80 after the outlier detection. It should be remembered that there is a hard limit
before estimating a pose of 15 for the SURF features and 8 for the Harris feature in the
next part.
Harris based estimation
Harris features are fundamental different from SURF features, as they describe features
as corner points, and SURF features describes blobs. In addition, then the LVS used
Harris operators to find the landmarks.

where it’s only differences between −0.05 and 0.1. The two other components spans
0.4, however this result is similar to the case of the SURF case, when the peak is not
included. The directional vectors can then be used to estimate the angle between the two
vectors, which is shown in Figure 6.12. One thing to note from this is that it’s actual higher
general compared to the SURF case, when the peak from the SURF case is excluded.
The average angle between the directional vectors is 15.9° ± 4.6°, which compared to
the SURF case is larger, but the standard deviation is far higher, which can be explained
by the peak. This is also visible in Figure 6.13a and Figure 6.13b, where the angles are
more or less equal to the SURF case, once again without the peak.

Comparison of SURF and Harris
In order to better make a comparison of the two features, then Table 6.1 can be used,
where the standard deviation and average of the differences are shown. It can be seen
that the average componets of the directional vector in the x­. and y­ directions is smaller
for the SURF features, which indicates that SURF might be good for this, however the
standard deviation is significant larger than Harris, where the reason behind this might be
the peak in Figure 6.3b. The average estimation error for the downward component (z) is
low in both cases which is quite good, where the standard deviation is over 3 times larger
for SURF than Harris. The angle between the estimated and true directional vectors is
unfortunately quite significant as the average angle is in both cases almost 16° where the
SURF has a standard deviation of 11.1° and the Harris have only a standard deviation
of 4.6°. The tendency to have a higher standard deviation for the SURF compared to
Harris shows that SURF doesn’t estimate the pose very good, as the standard deviation
can be seen as the precision and the average value being the accuracy. Thus, SURF
features might from the two first components of the directional vector have a better ac­
curacy, however, the precision is not great and can be seen as more chaotic estimations
compared to Harris. The azimuth angle however is more difficult to use as a good refer­
ence, as it’s highly coupled to the nadir angle, where larger nadir angles results in better
estimations of the azimuth angle due to the geometry. However, the nadir angle is far
more interesting as it can be used to see how well it determines the downward angle. It’s
likewise also coupled to the other components, but it far less coupled than the azimuth
angle. Here it can be seen that Harris have an average estimation error of 0.152° with
a standard deviation of 10.9°, which means thateven though that the accuracy is great,
then the precision isn’t as great. The SURF case shows over 10 times worse accuracy
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
75
==PAGE:92==
6.1. Mars 2020
and less precise (1.88° and 15.6°). Looking at the positional accuracy and precision, then
SURF features once again seems to have a better accuracy of the components, however
the 3D or Euclidean distance of the estimation errors shows that the Harris features is
actual 180 m better, which can indicate that the components might be far more chaotic,
which is confirmed when looking at the standard deviation, which is significant higher for
all the components for the SURF case, and likewise also the 3D estimation error. One
interesting result is that both cases of pose estimation are quite good at estimating the
z­component or height as the average and especially the standard deviation is quite low.
The signs of the y­ and z­ components furthermore shows that it’s underestimating these,
while the x­components are overestimated. When comparing the computations times,
then it’s quite surprising that the standard deviations are equal, however the Harris case
is around 50 % slower than SURF. Improving the time/speed requires additional improve­
ments and optimizations, like tuning the feature settings. However, using the current state
of the pose estimation is not recommended without having a correct calibrated camera, as
this is most likely the source of errors, as the used camera model only used the intrinsics
without the fish eye distortions, which actually make these results invalid to use, this was
the reason to have a better controlled environment in ONL.

it can be seen that for hills and craters, then there is a positive and negative side for the
slope components. In order to better use this, then the slope magnitude and direction can
be used to give some idea of the danger level. The slope magnitude and direction can be
found from Equation 3.28 and 3.29, which is shown in Figure 6.18, where both the direct
magnitude of the slope, the slope angle and direction are shown.

presented. Using solely orbital images to map hazards will generally provide a coarse
estimation of hazards, however smaller hazards such as rocks can not always be seen
from orbital images, where the hazards map onboard Mars 2020 used rock distribution
models in order to predict this. Using the images from the LCAM can provide images in
far better resolution and therefore open up to determining hazards ’live’. One of the use
cases here would be to improve the STS system as the spacecraft descending through
the atmosphere. However, to prove that they can be used in this case, then a series
of features is tested on the LCAM images, where the speed of the detection is showed
as well. In contrast to the method of comparing to an onboard map, then mapping the
hazards during the descent will require a fast system, which means that it should be fast.

Harris, KAZE and SIFT features are detected. In these figure, then the circles represent
the size of the features, where the corner point detection of the Harris features is only
point features. For the SURF features, then decreasing the metric threshold (strength
of the detected features, lower equals more features/blobs) results in far more features,

6.1. Mars 2020
The LCAM images with the numbers/ids 73,75,78 and the added features on top are
shown in Figure 6.20. For the first image(Figure 6.20a and 6.20b), then the shadow of the
spacecraft appears in the top left corner. However, it can be seen that the Harris features
are distributed very well and covers many of the surface features, except for what appears
to be ridges in the terrain. These will likely be covered in a DEM with greater resolution.
There appears to be no difference in choosing between these two settings of minimum
quality in this case. The next image is the 75th, which are shown in Figure 6.20c and
6.20d, where the shadow of the rover wheels can be seen more clearly, furthermore,
the rocket plume from the skycrane, for which the task was to put the Mars 2020 rover
down safely on the surface. In this image then the smaller rocks are more visible and
the Harris feature points seems to detect most of these. This however will be too late to
select a whole other landing site, however it would help to land where fewest of these
rocks are located in order to reduce the risk of the rocket plume tossing rocks into the
rover. One thing to note at this point with the rocket plume, is that there may be features
which aren’t hazards but rather just the rocket plume. In the third image, Figure 6.20e
and 6.20f, then the wheels of the rover are deployed, which also shows that the camera is
located near the wheels. As of this point, then there is not much that can be done to avoid
any rocks, as it appears to be very close to landing on the surface. Due to the effects
of the rocket plume, then features can not be used to find hazards at this point, as the
rocket plume disturbs the surface too much. It can be seen that overall feature detection
time is around 100 ms in these image and remembering that the image from far above
the surface took 113 ms, then the overall process will be sufficient to get an idea of where
these are in the image, however, the conversion to the actual coordinate refenfe like the
onboard hazard amp should be account too, which requires image processing which as
well will take additional time, otherwise the features can be tracked live and then estimated
from using triangulation where the different images can be used as the additional image
views when the pose of the camera is known. Another interesting thing about the figures
is that when the rover gets near to the surface then the difference between having a
higher/lower minimum quality threshold is dimishing, where therefore shows this doesn’t
matter as much when there is enough features/details that clearly stands out from the
surrounding terrain.
Comparison to the reference hazards map
In order to compare how hazards found from the slopes and features to the onboard
hazards map of Mars 2020, then a cropped region near the landing site is selected. This
is shown in Figure 6.21, where its landing spot is marked as a purple diamond on the
bottom, where the alternative landing spot in the prime wedge (upper wedge) is marked
as well. Here the different parameters of the STS are shown (matching between maps
and reference frames, path and the definition of the zero­divert point), however, this is not
important here, and is described in the paper. This map can then be converted to grey­
scaled, in order to have overlays which are coloured similar. The overlays can be seen
in Figure 6.22, where the slopes with the custom danger levels are shown in the colour
of subsubsection 6.1.2. Furthermore, Harris and KAZE features are shown as well, since
they detect many of these features. It should be noted that the pixel scales that they are
done at is 6 m/pixel, and therefore the features aren’t as dense as the LCAM images.
By comparing Figure 6.21 and 6.22a, then it’s obvious that the exact colouring isn’t equal,
furthermore it noted that the onboard hazard map is padded such that the slopes and
direct hazards such as rocks are combined. However, looking at the two figures, then
they fit quite good as many of the slopes are marked as red, and that craters mostly are
marked red­orange. This shows that the challenging slopes indeed can be found from
the DEM, however, one should always make an inspection on any of these maps prior

6.2.1
Calibrations
In order to use the stereo cameras, then they must be calibrated before imaging. This
is done over a calibration pattern, where several images are captured. Then this is im­
ported into MATLABs stereo calibration tool, where it was selected to return three radial
coefficients, the skewness and the tangential distortion coefficients as well. The result
can be seen in Equation 6.1, where the left figure shows the orientation of the stereo
camera relative to the calibration pattern, and to the right, the reprojection errors, where
the mean is error is 0.94 pixel which is very good. This exact camera models can be seen
in Table B.2, where all the different parameters are shown. Here it can be seen that the
focal length is 18.3 mm, which fits nicely to the actual focal length of the lens which was
set to 18 mm. Furthermore, it can be seen that the skewness angle is very low and the
pixel size on either side is almost equal, which means that the pixels are squared.
