Chapter 6. Discussion and presentation of results
6.2.2
Reference map evaluation
As the map is key to a well pose estimation, then the generated maps should be evaluated.
In order to evaluate the quality of the generated maps, then they are visually inspected.
For the first versions of the maps, then discontinuities and shifts in the assigned pixels
appeared in multiple places, an example was shown in Figure 4.11. This was reduced by
introducing the edge weight for the individual maps, which corrected the discontinuities,
and by introducing a correction to the skewness of the reference system as mentioned in
subsection 4.3.4, then the shifts in assigned pixels were eliminated as well. By comparing
the new map to this older version, then it can be seen that there have been several im­
provements, and that the quality seems very good here. The shifts in the assigned pixels
was more challenging to find the source of this, however, by inputs from meeting with
Mathias, then the caused was found by a skewness in the reference system relative to
the terrain. However, the exact cause still remains unknown, however corrections were
made to counteract this, and therefore eliminate this map error.
6.2.3
Pose accuracy
The follow subparts will be briefly presented, as the discussion and overall evaluation of
the developed algorithm will be done later in this part.
Mapping grid ­ Narrower ­ SURF features with metric threshold of 1000
In order to visualize the results from the pose estimation algorithm, then positions of the
robotic arm similar to the used route from the one used to the mapping algorithm can be
used to find the performance efficiently. Obviously, it should be noted that the grid was
narrower than the one used by the mapping algorithm, and therefore different images.
The estimations are done by both having a cropped and uncropped reference map and
then using a binned or the full image of the test series, where the combinations can be
used to estimate the advantages and disadvantages by choosing one of these versions.
The following part will include the figures from the case with a metric threshold of 1000,
the other cases are included in Table 6.6, and figures are shown in the appendix.
Euler angle
estimations
The estimated angles of the camera can be seen in Figure 6.24, where the ’true’ angles
from the robotic arm are shown as well which in this case zero. This can as well be viewed
differently by looking at the difference between the true and the estimated angles, which
is shown in Figure 6.25, which should be equal to the angle estimations as the angle of
the robotic arm remain zero (looking downward).
The differences are then summed up in the Table 6.2, where the key values are noted
such as the standard deviation, and mean value. From this table and Figure 6.25, then
it’s obvious that A(rotation around the z­axis) is estimated quite well, as the mean value
is very close to zero (under an arcminute). The standard deviation is quite similar for the
two other angles, where the non­binned images are slightly lower than the binned images.
The highest estimation error can be seen in the C angle, where the mean value is greater
than 0.3°. Furthermore, it can be seen that using the full images (no binning) results in
the best estimates, where the effect of the setting of using a cropped reference image can
not be determined from this data.
Directional vectors
estimation
Another way of visualizing the orientation of the camera, is directional vectors, just like
Mars 2020, where the benefits are that the pointing direction and the individual com­
ponents isn’t cross coupled as the Euler angles. The procedure to find these directional

vectors is to use the rotation matrix by using the euler angles, and then test with a unit vec­
tor along the z­axis to get an idea of the pointing direction. This is shown in.Figure 6.26,
where the directional components are shown, and the true direction have only a direc­
tional component in the z­direction pointing downward. This can then be used to find the
difference as in Figure 6.27, where it’s not surprising that it’s very similar to the other fig­
ure, as the true components are constant. These directional vectors can be used to find
the actual angle between these vectors to give an estimate on how well aligned they are.
This is shown in Figure 6.28, where it can be seen that the binned images deviates more
from zero than the non­binned images. The average deviation can be seen in Table 6.3,
where the average estimation error is equal to 0.359° to 0.374° for the non­binned im­
ages, compared to over 0.42° for the binned images, this means that non­binned images
seems to work best, which aren’t a surprise by knowing that by binning, then details are
lost.

Another way of visualizing the orientation is to use the directional vectors to estimate the
azimuth and nadir angle, which unfortunately are cross­coupled. However, for this series
of images, then it’s pointing direction should be vertical down, meaning a nadir angle
of 0°. If the nadir angle is close to zero, then it can be expected that any small deviation
along the x and y component from zero will result in larger deviations in the azimuth angle.

Therefore, the main interest of the two angles here is the nadir angle. In Figure 6.29, then
the estimated azimuth and nadir angles are shown in addition of the true angles. Here
it can be seen that the camera is pointing downward throughout the images, where the
nadir angle is within 0.5° when using the unbinned version of the images, while the binned
versions are up to 1°. The azimuth angle here is not useful, as it can be any value, as the
nadir angle is zero. However, it can show in which direction that the estimation errors are
pointing. It should be noted that the nadir angle will be between zero and 180° as it’s found
by looking at the plane span by the z­component and the directional vector, and then the
angle between the two vectors can be found by simple geometry of triangles. It can be
seen from Figure 6.30 that it overestimates the angle by around 90°, which corresponds
to a direction of the negative y­axis. To visualize this better, then Figure 6.31 can be
used as it shows the pointing direction as seen from above (at the centre of the plot).
The horizontal and vertical axes are similar to the reference system, such as the azimuth
angle of 0° points in the direction of x, and 90° in the direction of y. Here it can be seen
that the estimation is mainly pointing in the negative y direction and therefore explains the
88
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 6. Discussion and presentation of results
higher deviation of the azimuth. Furthermore, using this figure, one can get an impression
on how precise the estimation is visually, as a higher precision means that the points are
more densely located. This is very visible when comparing the binned to the unbinned
images, since the binned images are more spread with nadir angles of almost 1°. By
looking at the table of Table 6.3, then it can be seen that the nadir angles are equal to
that of the angle between the directional vectors. This is because of the reference point
direction is downward at a nadir angle of zero, and any deviation from this will give an
angle deviation with a direction corresponding to the azimuth.

of around 1.4 mm for the unbinned images and 2 mm for the binned images.
Time and matched points
performance parameters
Another interesting parameter, to compare, is the time it takes to compute the poses from
images. Figure 6.35 shows the computation time, that it takes to estimate the pose when
using either a binned or normal image as input, and using the reference image cropped
or uncropped. An interesting thing to notice from the plot, is that the full scale image(non­
binned) versions takes a significant longer timer than when the input images are binned.
Especially when the reference image is uncropped. Compared to binned images, then
there is a significant drawback to using the full scale input images when looking solely
on the computation time. Another view into the data can be seen in Figure 6.36, where
the amount of matched points shows another view. Compared to the computation time,

between orientation and position estimation errors

of cross coupling between the angles and the position estimation errors. In order to see
this more clearly, then the correlation coefficients can be found by correlating the different
components. This was done in MATLAB by using corrcoef on a matrix with the different
components as columns.

The correlation made from cropping the reference image and using the full scale test
images (no binning) can be seen in Figure 6.37, where the two variants are just the cor­
relation coefficient as they are with sign, and then the absolute correlation coefficients
without the sign. For Figure 6.37a, it can be seen that the diagonal is of course 1, as
each component should correlate perfectly with itself. Furthermore, looking at the scale,
then either white or black coloured boxes have a high correlation; positively and nega­
tively correlated. This can as well be looked at the strength of correlation which is just
the values without the sign, thus the colouring of the boxes can be changes, so white
boxes indicates strongly correlation, and black means weakly correlation. Here it can be
seen that ∆y and ∆C correlates very well(−0.99), like ∆x and ∆B(−0.97). These are
negatively correlated mean that as the ∆B or ∆C increases, then respective ∆x or ∆y
decreases. This corresponds very well to the reference frame of the robotic arm and the
Euler angles, where A corresponds to a rotation about the z/h axis, B is a rotation about
the y axis, and lastly C is a rotation about the x axis, meaning that by following the rotation
order, then the same correlation will be found. It should however be noted that if there
isn’t a strong correlation as the mentioned strong correlations of strengths above 0.9, then
the correlation can as well be minor cross couplings or simply just noise, as the amount

estimation errors should be uncorrelated, as that otherwise will show up as a some kind
of dependence which means that there is a systematic error, which could be explained
by the corrections. When looking at Figure 6.38, then the two similar correlation matrices
can be used to see if there is some correlation which shouldn’t be there. It can be seen
that there is some kind of correlation between the x­component and the estimation error

The overview
of the image series
In order to show how great the pose estimation is, then Figure 6.39 shows how a single
image (20th in the order), in the top right corner was estimated to be at the red camera
as seen on the other figures. When looking at the camera from the top­down image, then
the centre looks very similar to the image, which confirms that at least the coarse pose
estimation worked very well. In addition to this figure, then Figure 6.40 can be used to get
an idea of all the images from this series and where they are placed, both in the form of
a top­down image and 3D image. In this figure, then the cyan coloured cameras are the
uncorrected reference poses of the camera given, which are included to show where they
are in relation to the robotic arm. The black cameras are the corrected reference poses
where the correction has been added. Here it’s visible that the correction increases as
y increases. The red cameras show the estimated poses above the terrain. The reason
that they are not converted directly into the reference frame of the robotic reference frame
is that the map is based on the added corrections in order to combine the individual maps,
which is a drawback on how the correction was done.
Test series with different angles
As the previous test series was done with a constant z­component and orientation down­
ward, then different orientations and positions of the camera can be used to give an idea
of the limitations with the algorithm and the experimental setup, and to show problems
with the current method. The following pose estimation is done with SURF features with
the metric threshold of 2500, for the additional speed.
The test series consist of 5 different series:

• 4Points alt ­ 4 points which resemble some sort of descent in the crater filled area

• Angles ­ Different angles to find relations between angles
To show the different series, then Figure 6.41 can be used to see where the true/reference
is located for the angles and the positions. This figure, in combination with the estimation
difference from the reference as seen in Figure 6.42, can be used to see where the limi­
tations of the algorithm exist, and where additional works are needed in order to compare
accuracy. It should be noted that cross­coupling between angles and positions can still
be a problem, so one should remember this when looking at the figures. As it can be seen
when comparing Figure 6.41 and 6.42, then it can be seen that the angles gets estimated
quite well compared to that the tests involves several different angles. It can be seen that
the third image in the 3Points series (image no. 3) isn’t estimated when using a binned
version of the that image, furthermore as it can be seen from Figure 6.41a, the angle C
was also quite high at this image. This series does seem however not to be estimated

6.2. Test setup
Figure 6.40: Positions of the image series pointing downward, where the cyan coloured
camera corresponds to the true uncorrected poses of the cameras, the black cameras
are the corrected true poses of the cameras, the red cameras are the estimated poses.
This is both shown as 2D(top­down image) and in 3D. This is done with SURF features
and MT of 1000
as good as the other series, which may indicate that C have higher influence where the
algorithm isn’t as robust. There also seems to be a clear correlation with the angle of B
and the height h of the camera, as the estimated height h have estimation errors which
fits the true B and h. Here it should be remembered that the camera is in a stereo setup,
where the rotational centre is located between the two cameras. This offset from the
rotational/pivot centre result in a change in the height of the camera and slightly in the
y­axis (due to the geometric coupling). This can be solved by using the reference angles
and position, and then the calibration between the two cameras, where the distance is
approximately 200 mm, where the rotational centre is 100 mm towards the x­direction of
the left primary camera. This was corrected to some degree by adding 100 mm to the
x­component from the rotational center offset from the robotic arm and then using the
rotation of angles Equation 6.2 from section C.2, which is seen in Figure 6.43. It can be
seen from the remnant error seen in ∆h, that there may be some additional corrections
needed for this rotation, which shows that the alignment and position of the camera on the
robotic arm is clearly a source of error, where the exact offset from the rotational centre
should be studied further to get better estimates, as the simple offset of 100 mm in the

that the point direction is estimated quite well.

Discussion of the results
The results from the narrower mapping grid can be seen in Table 6.6, where the different
feature settings of SURF were used. In addition, then Table 6.5 shows a slower feature.
When the metric threshold for the SURF feature is decreased to be below the default of
1000, then more feature points will be detected which in the end will have an impact on
the performance. This tendency is clearly seen in the case of the mean average compu­
tation time for this image series, where the slowest combination was the using the entire
reference image (uncropped) and the full image which hasn’t been binned. This averaged
to 342 s with a standard deviation of 184 s, compared to the fastest of that feature setting
with binned imaged and cropped reference image, which is only 4.12 s with a standard de­
viation of 3.49 s. However, using a binned image has the drawback of being less accurate,
which is seen both for the orientation and position compared to using the full resolution of
the image, and also appearing at all the different SURF metric thresholds. There is a dif­
102
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 6. Discussion and presentation of results
ference of around 1.5 mm between the best and worst performing version, which clearly
shows that binned images shall only be used for the speed advantage. Looking at the
whatever the reference image shall be cropped, then there isn’t a clear advantage in se­
lecting the uncropped version, as the accuracy more or less stay the same. On the other
hand, choosing the cropped version clearly gives a speed bonus. Therefore, a cropped
version of the reference image will provide faster estimates without the penality of addi­
tional time. This makes sense when knowing that the images should overlap nicely, but
having several other features which naturally won’t be present in the image would just
make the computation of matches far slower, so this can be seen as the prime filtration
that will provide the best speed improvement. Comparing to the other features settings,
then it’s clearly seen that using a higher metric threshold does affect the accuracy to
some degree, however this doesn’t compare to the speed improvement that fewer points
will yield. To compare the between the feature setting of 850 and the faster 2500, then the
difference in the mean in the orientation is only 0.027° which is under 2 arcminutes, and
0.007° difference in the standard deviation. The corresponding difference in the position
mean error is 0.29 mm with 0.05 mm in the standard deviation. That is a very small differ­
ence, compared to achieving this kind of performance while being around 13 times faster.
One could also other different metric thresholds, however, the tendency is that increasing
the threshold decreases the accuracy, and in this study, then the breaking point of where
the accuracy decreases too much while the speed increases has not been studied, but
this is highly relevant to study, as some optimization can be done here. One thing to
note about these accuracy errors, is that the sources are expected to be misalignment
errors both in the relation to the test series, but also in the making of the DTM. Thus, the
correction made, may have had other impacts on the map that were not intended, which
may explain the rather high accuracy errors of several mm along all three components.
However, overall, as it can be expected, then using the binned images, have a cost on
the accuracy and precision, but generally faster, where the unbinned and therefore full
scale images are a bit slower but provides the best estimates.

but this requires rerunning the algorithm several times.

Additional work
To test the pose estimation algorithm for robustness against different lighting conditions,
then a test series was done in the first part of this project, where a different robotic pro­
gram was used, so the reference coordinates will have offsets. However, in this setup, a
spotlight was placed at the end of the table(at rail=0 mm), which cast shadows over the
terrain. This test was firstly intended for mapping purposes, but the shadows were shad­
ing many of the features. While testing of this on the made map, then it returned under
10 matches for every image, which meant that they were rejected automatically. This can
be seen in Figure 6.49, where it actually only returns matches (before the final rejection)
that are indeed not all matches, which on the other hand confirms that the rejection/lower
limit of matches works. The reason behind this is that the contrast may be too high as the
shadows and spotlight makes the terrain either very dark or bright, as the sand reflects
the light.
Figure 6.49: Example of the matching of features with an image where a spotlight is
positioned such that the lwoer part of the image is strongly illuminated and the background
from the hill is shadowed

6.2.4
Quality of the terrain and implication on the results ­ sand grains etc.
In addition to the results presented here, then coarser reference maps were tested for the
first versions of the algorithm, which showed that having for example a pixel scale corre­
sponding to 0.5 mm/pixel will provide an okay result when pointing directly at the surface,
but the errors are significant when an angle is introduced, which makes sense when the
details of the camera corresponds to a pixel scale of around 0.13 mm/pixel (found from
comparing the distance between two points in both the test and reference image), and
therefore corresponds to a significant decrease in details. This wasn’t included in this re­
port as the map generated was made with slightly different algorithm where there weren’t
any corrections, so it can be expected to better if generated with the current state of the
algorithm, but this is similar to the case of Mars 2020, where it was seen that the pose
estimations only worked when the pixel scales were better matching, as high altitudes
results in LCAM images having sizes of pixels covering larger areas where the reference
image cover an area of 6m × 6m, where at lower altitudes than it’s the opposite situation,
where there are too many details compared to the reference image. Using features will
however provide some robustness for some features that are more scale invariant, but
pixel scales should still be similar in order to match with the correct scales. For the spe­
cific map used in the test setup, then it can be seen from the images, that individual sand
grains does have an impact, which a real reference map of a planetary surface wouldn’t
have. Therefore, this will have to be taken into account when directly comparing the two
cases, as the surface is very different.
6.3
Evaluation of the results to set up
requirements/limitations for such a system
It can be seen from the results that the algorithm have limitations regarding higher angles
from downward (nadir). This was especially seen for the 3Points series in image series
of different angles Therefore, a coarse set of limitation for pose estimation algorithm for
106
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 6. Discussion and presentation of results
the case of the own map, can be seen below:
• C < 20°
• Position should be within the map region
The two other angles should be tested more, to see the direct implications on the accuracy,
but it was seen that there were a cross coupling between the B angle and the estimated
height h. Therefore, there should be looked more into corrections for the rotational pivot
point of the robotic arm, which should aid in the process of finding the exact limits as this
will be a systematic error if not correct for fully. Furthermore, it should be investigated how
sensitive the algorithm is to changing lighting conditions. Therefore, to get more concise
limits, then many more different orientations and positions in different lighting conditions
should be done. This will also be the way to improve the algorithm by investigating some
of the ’edge’ cases where the algorithm most likely will struggle.
6.3.1
Perspectives to how it can be validated and verified
In order to validate and verify the algorithm, then similar procedure as the algorithm for
Mars 2020 could be used, as mentioned in section D.2. This, however, would be quite
cumbersome and not worth it for its current state, as pose estimation should be both faster
and more accurate. Another, and better way, is to test an artificial generated terrain in
computer software, and then simulate different views which can be done quite well where
misalignments between the robotic arm and camera can be reduced to zero. This would
also make it possible to test in different light conditions and using different surface proper­
ties. For the specific test setup, then several more angles and positions can be tested, this
however would require that the alignment between the robotic rotation point and the cam­
era is estimated and modelled correctly. If possible, having the image capturing process
being automated with image capturing and notation of the robotic arm would be beneficial,
as one can expect a long image series, especially if different lighting conditions are tested
as well, where manually capturing many hundreds of images would time­consuming.
6.4
Wrap­up
To wrap up on the results from this project, then it was seen that the pose estimation
algorithm did work on both the Mars 2020 dataset and the ONL, as an estimated pose
was provided by the algorithm for most images. The images which didn’t work were ones
where the image pixel scales doesn’t match the reference image, as in the case of Mars
2020 or simply that the images didn’t resemble each other very well. In the case of Mars
2020, then it was seen that poses were heavily affected by errors, as the errors in the po­
sition were several hundreds of meters off, and the angle of the directional vectors were
approximately 16° off, which isn’t great. However, this may as well be caused by the sim­
plifications of not correcting for the fish eye lens effects. This shows how important it is
to have a known camera model of a calibrated camera that can be used in the algorithm.
One important thing to note, was that the Mars 2020 images required a transformation
between the MCMF reference frame to epirectangular projection, which most likely will
contain some errors, as this isn’t well described in the literature of how the different ref­
erence frames are related exactly in this case. When testing in the ONL case, then the
pose algorithm seemed to be working quite better for this dataset where it manages to be
within 5 mm ± 2 mm for the poses looking downward, and 16.5 mm ± 9 mm for the test
series of multiple angles and positions. The latter accuracy for the test series could be
explained in terms of the offset of the camera from the robotic arm’s pivoting points, as this
was only corrected to some degree. For the pointing accuracy, then both the downward
looking series, and the series with multiple angles estimated the orientation to be around
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
107
==PAGE:124==
6.5. Future work
0.5°, where the standard deviation for the downward looking series was 0.2°, and for the
other series was 0.4°
It was furthermore seen that the feature types that worked for the Mars 2020 dataset,
were the Harris and SURF features, as it high number of matches. Harris performed
better in terms of precision, where in some cases, SURF features gave a better accuracy
while being less precise. However, in the ONL case, it was seen early in that using other
feature types, result in fewer matches if any, which is not ideal. Instead, SURF features
seem to work quite good in this case, as a match is found for every image without being
too imprecise, this can be seen in Figure A.3 where the matched points are located at the
same spot in both images. SURF features do have different settings, where the focus in
this project was tuning the metric threshold, which makes the detection algorithm return
more features by decreasing this threshold. Tuning the SURF features settings wasn’t the
only way of improving the speed, as different versions of input images were made. The
first two versions contains the normal test image (no binning) and then having a cropped
or uncropped reference image. For the Mars 2020 case, then the testruns were only
done with the cropping functionality. The two other versions tests the effect of binning
the test images, like LCAM did by binning by 2 × X. Here it was seen that binning the
images greatly improves the time, but accuracy will likewise decrease, which does make
sense when binning actually reduces the spatial information. Overall, the speed was
fairly good for the Mars 2020 compared to being programmed in MATLAB, where the
speed improvements when porting to for instance C++, will result in a significant speed
improvement, as the code in MATLAB tends to use more memory than what is actually
needed. Comparing this to the achieved pose estimation accuracy of Mars 2020, makes
this speed less impressive, as the average time was 1 s to 1.5 s. The test setup worked far
better with the pose estimation, as achieved an average speed of 2s with accuracies of a
couple millimetres (corresponds to around 30 times the pixel scale of the reference image)
for the first test, compared to the 1 s where the accuracy was an average of a kilometre for
the Mars 2020 (corresponds to around 150 times the pixel scale of the reference image).
The ONL case did worse on the image series with the many different angles, where the
additional time can be caused by using several challenging angles, and the less accurate
estimation is mainly due to the offset of the camera compared to the pivot point of the
robotic arm. By comparing the speed to the accuracy of the point vectors, then it can be
seen that the pose estimation on the test setup works very well, as the average offset
was 0.5° compared to over 10° in the case of the Mars 2020. However, as it has been
stated then the algorithms is developed from the ideas of a calibrated camera, where the
intrinsics and distortions are known, and that the lighting conditions is not changing, where
the case of ONL confirms that algorithm does work very good as the accuracy is very high
compared to the sources of errors from the corrections made. Mars 2020 likewise, proves
that a calibrated camera is indeed needed, as the LCAM have a high distortion.
6.5
Future work
As the project progressed, it was seen that several parameters should be tuned and opti­
mized. Furthermore, other methods of pose estimation and generation of reference maps
exist which hasn’t been implemented or studied in details, which could be better. In this
section, then, these things will be discussed and how they relate to this project.
6.5.1
Tuning and optimizing the pose algorithm
As it can be seen from the results of the pose algorithm, then different parameters can be
tuned. This is especially the features and their internal settings, which returns different
amount of features. It was seen that some features matched greatly, but others not, which
108
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 6. Discussion and presentation of results
indicates that this could be studied in more details.
6.5.2
Pivoting point of the robotic arm
It was seen that rotating the camera induces errors in the pose estimation, as the cameras
are mounted in the stereo setup, meaning an offset to the pivoting point is introduced for
every angle. This can be solved by either modelling this, or placing the stereo cameras
differently. One way to do this, is to have the main camera mounted such that the pivoting
point is at the camera centre, such that the second stereo camera sticks out to the other
side. This will make it easier to use the images without having to correct for the rotation
of the robotic arm, without the downside of being out of balance.
6.5.3
Terrain should be less grainy
Since the main difference between the Mars 2020 dataset and the test made was on the
scale, then this scaling might have a bigger difference than anticipated, due to the visible
sand grains in the test series. Due to these grains, then many non­unique features can
be detected as it isn’t just pure noise. This puts an extra load of matching features, where
the case of the Mars 2020 had a much smoother surface, where the hills etc. could be
differentiated better. This, however, requires that the terrain can be made with finer sand
grains, where David Pedersen from DTU Space have mentioned that some upper surface
material can be applied which resembles more like the real surface of f.ex. Mars with the
albedo etc. which could be used to better simulate real images.
6.5.4
Improve the method of making the reference maps
It was seen that some problems appeared when making the reference map. These prob­
lems were primarily due to the combining of the different individual maps, and therefore
this step could be improved. There are two ways to improve the reference maps; making
the methods more robust or make sure that the setup is 100 % aligned. The first way to
improve the method, is to make the method robust against misalignments. This should
be done, as small misalignment can always be expected. This could be done by either
making sure that the reference frame from the robotic arm is aligned with the camera,
or by matching points between different positions. This could for instance be done with
bundle adjustment. Bundle adjustment generally adjust both the points and the camera
poses, which however requires that the camera poses remain fixed to the robotic arm
and therefore that the alignment between the cameras and robotic reference frame is well
aligned or at least known. Therefore, aligning the cameras and robotic reference frame
should be the first step to improve this method. However, one could also use GIS pro­
grams which can generate the reference maps, however a quick search on the common
GIS programs shows that they either can’t use custom reference frames, or that they re­
quire some advanced and custom inputs into the programs. The benefit of these GIS
programs, however, is that a stereo setup isn’t strictly necessary, as the images should
have reference coordinates included. Knn search men i forskellige retninger
6.5.5
Test on an artificial generated terrain in 3D software
Another way of making a controllable environment, is to implement one in 3D software
and thereby be able to make a specific surface structure and texturing it with realistic
properties. From this, then the mapping could be done to test this and get an estimate on
how well this was done compared to the true 3D surface, which is rather difficult with a
terrain made of sand. Furthermore, different lighting conditions could be simulated to test
many different situations. However, this wasn’t done in this project as it’s a whole other
subject on modelling and texturing a terrain, and afterwards simulate different views. This
way will be the best to get as much data as possible, and if the algorithm was optimized
further and ported to another programming language, then the mapping could likely be
done ’live’ in the 3D software.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
109
==PAGE:126==
6.5. Future work
6.5.6
Test at different lighting conditions
As the title suggest, then testing of different lighting conditions should be done, as this is
necessary to make it able to work in real life. This could be done by placing a dimmed
spotlight at different orientations and then changing light output as well. However, once
again this should be done when the code has been optimized further to reduce the memory
load, as a test series at different poses, with different orientations of the light, at different
light outputs would result in a very big database of images. So here it would be better if
it could run ’live’ such that the estimated and reference pose of the camera are the only
kept data. LVS was tested with a wide range of lighting conditions, where the position of
the sun was included as well.
6.5.7
Optimizations ­ code language
The developed algorithm is based on MATLAB code, which does have limitations on per­
formance regarding speed. According to a study by Tyler [40], then it was shown that for
the specific task in that study (Delta V calculation), C++ was around 500 times faster than
MATLAB when using the C++ compiled code in MATLAB(MEX). This difference is mostly
due to the MATLAB scripting and functions which are compiled every time, where C++
compiles the code one time and then run the programs, as functions from libraries are
compiled in one go. Furthermore, MATLAB has a tendency of using the double variable
type which takes a considerable amount of memory which decreases the performance.
Ideally, then only the estimated and reference poses should be kept between different
images, but at the current state, then keeping the same data, were deemed to be better
for the ability to look closer in the data and quickly redo parts if needed. Another benefit
of porting the MATLAB code to for instance C++, is that it can be easier to implement on
dedicated hardware.
6.5.8
Datasets
In order to improve the developed algorithm, then more data is required. And in this case,
also more different cases, such as the Moon. It was unfortunately not possible to locate
good data from this other than test images from Apollo 15, and a high quality DEM, other
it would have been interesting to see how this algorithm performs for this case.
6.5.9
Implement EKF for simulating how it’s typical will be used
Mars 2020 used an EKF to take full advantage of an onboard IMU and the LCAM. It would
be interesting to see how much this will improve the results if an IMU can be simulated
with its uncertainties and then combined with the pose estimation algorithm. This will most
likely result in better estimations, and the error sources could be changed to test different
scenarios.
6.5.10
CAHVORE model
In order to take full advantage of the great and detailed Mars 2020 dataset, then the
CAHVORE model must be implemented, such that it could be used. If this was to be
corrected, then it’s expected to reduce most of the estimation errors of the pose in the
Mars 2020 dataset. This, however, could also be used for other projects using this data,
as information of the application of CAHVORE, is very sparse on the internet.
110
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 7. Conclusion
7
Conclusion
This study, in the field of optical navigation for planetary surfaces, has shown that feature
based matching of camera images and a reference image, can be used for pose estima­
tion in this field. In the case of Mars 2020, the developed algorithm managed to estimate
with an accuracy of 1.06 km for Harris features, where the LVS, which was the system
onboard the rover, achieved a landing accuracy of just 5 m. This rather big difference is
most likely caused by the missing camera model of the fish eye lens, where the CAHVORE
model was used by the LCAM. In the case of the test done in ONL, then the terrain made in
sand, had to be mapped, as this is required for the developed method. This digital model
of the terrain was made with stereo imaging to extract the height and then processed to
form the surface. That involved triangulating surface points, and interpolating to a smooth
3D surface where 3D points were reprojected into the image to extract the pixels, for mak­
ing an orthophoto. This task was more complex than anticipated, as sources of errors had
to be taken care of or be identified. That involved corrections to the reference coordinates
and optimizing the merging of individual maps into a combined map. As a result, a map
covering 1 m2 was made with a spatial resolution of 0.15 mm. This was used with a series
of test images that points downward, to get an accuracy and precision of 5 mm ± 2 mm,
and the orientation accuracy and precision was within 0.4° ± 0.2° on average. Another
test series was done with several other angles and positions, where the pose estimation
estimated the position to be 16.5 mm±9 mm, and orientation 0.5°±0.4°. Therefore, from
this it was shown that the pose estimation worked as intended, however, a quick look
into images with other lighting conditions revealed that the pose estimation didn’t work
as intended here, which means that there is indeed room for improvement. In order to
improve this further for real­life applications, then the code has to be optimized for speed
and made more robust for different lighting conditions, and in the end being ported to C++
or similar for the speed bonus and flexibility of implementation on dedicated hardware.
But to end this report, then

Appendix B. Tables
B
Tables
B.1
Requirements for Mars 2020 reference map
The requirements for making the map to Mars 2020 as stated from [37]
Requirement
Description
Map generation
The LVS map shall be a seamless and gap­free map composed of an LVS elevation map and co­registered
LVS appearance map over at least the expected 99.7 %tile landed ellipse determined by the time of the final
site selection workshop with a buffer that extends 8 km radially beyond the boundary of the landed ellipse.
Map projection
The LVS map shall use an “orthographic projection” with square pixels of equal size to the local tangent plane
of the Mars spheroid at the geometric center of the map.
Map elevation definition
The LVS elevation map shall define elevation as the perpendicular distance from Mars surface to the map
local tangent plane.
Map coregistration
The LVS elevation and appearance maps shall be horizontally co­registered to within 6 m (99 %) across the
30 km × 30 km LVS map.
Appearance map pixel size
The LVS appearance map shall be 6 m/pixel and be derived from imagery with an optical resolution of no
more than 6 m/pixel.
Elevation map pixel size
The LVS elevation map shall be 12 m/pixel and be derived from DEMs with a ground sample distance of no
more than 20 m/pixel.
Map absolute horizontal map tie error
The LVS map shall have an absolute horizontal error in position in the map tangent plane that is less than
300 m with respect to the P inertial coordinate frame.
Map absolute orientation error
The LVS map shall have an angular error around the normal to the map tangent plane at the center of the
map that is less than 1 mrad.
Horizontal map distortion
The LVS map shall have a horizontal distortion between any two pixels on the LVS map less than: 9 m (99 %)
for distances less than 120 m between pixels and 18 m (99 %) for all distances greater than or equal to 120 m
between pixels, up to 12 km.
Elevation map absolute elevation error
The LVS elevation map shall have an absolute vertical elevation error less than 20 m (3σ) with respect to
Mars Orbiter Laser Altimeter (MOLA) aeroid.
Elevation map relative elevation error
The LVS elevation map shall have a relative pixel to pixel elevation error less than 20 m (3σ).
Appearance map signal to noise ratio
The LVS appearance map shall preserve the SNR of the original radiometric corrected orbital images used
to construct the appearance map
Appearance map pre­processing
The LVS appearance map shall be made from images that are radiometrically corrected but not enhanced,
color balanced or smoothed to remove seams.
Table B.1: Requirements for Mars 2020 reference map[37]

S­curve derivatives
The s­curve in Equation C.1(4.4), is said to have the derivatives at x = 0 and x = 1 be
zero. This can be confirmed by differentiating the equation. It’s quite obvious that if x < 0
and x > 1 is differentiated, then the derivatives is zero.
f = 0
x < 0
f = 6 x5 −15 x4 + 10 x3
1 >= x >= 0
f = 1
x > 1
(C.1)
The derivative of the function f(x) = 6 x5 −15 x4 + 10 x3 is shown in Equation C.2. When
inserting x = 0 or x = 1 results in f′(0) = 0 and f′(1) = 0. Similarly, the second order
derivative is shown in Equation C.2, where it once again can be seen that f′′(0) = 0
and f′′(1) = 0. Furthermore, it can also be seen that the function is continuous, as the
derivatives are zero at the limits of the function where it meets the constant 0 or 1. This
will result in a smooth curve.

that a point can be rotated into a different orientation, or using the rotation matrix as the
transformation from one reference system into another. Using the rotation matrix as a
combination will generate a single matrix, where the number of matrix multiplications is
reduced significantly. In this relation, then Euler angles can be used to define the angles
of rotation for an object compared to a fixed reference system. However, the order of
the rotation does have an effect on the final rotation matrix, as well as the angles, where
situations may occur where information is lost (at angles near 90°, which can be noted as
gimbal lock.)

Kabsch Algorithm

section of this report.
Step 1 ­ Centroids
The centroids are basically just the average point of the N 3D points. These are funda­
mental to this algorithm, and shown in Equation D.1.

D.2
What has been accomplished ­ Focus on Mars 2020 LVS
Over several years, TRN has been studied and improved over a long time span, due
to the high interest in planetary landings by commercial companies and governmental
organizations like NASA/JPL. Most of these technologies relies on correlating between a
reference and descent image, however, can this exact procedure pose some challenges
that the technologies must be able to handle. These challenges involve shadowing of
crater rims, changing lighting conditions. According to [1], then newer generations of
TRN development may also use machine learning to be more robust for different lighting
conditions and seasons, which would be needed if a human outpost is going to happen.
Currently, Mars 2020 have proved that TRN can be used to land precise by giving precise
pose estimates by using LVS. LVS is designed as bolt­on equipment, meaning that it
leverages the benefit of not putting heavy loads on the spacecrafts onboard computer
and therefore can be used on many different spacecrafts. Vision based navigation is
much more computational heavy than simple GNSS, since there is much more data that
needs to be filtered and processed. LVS consist of two main operational modes; coarse
and fine, the following descriptions will be based on Mars 2020. A note to this part is
that the complete description of the system has only been available in the last part of the
project period, meaning that information has been limited in how to treat the distortions
etc. during the beginning of the thesis. Some parts were however clarified during this
period to some degree, as email correspondences with Andrew Johnson from JPL and
David Mayer from USGS, has provided important information on how to get the correct
images and what feature types that have been used. Furthermore, it was confirmed that
the fish­eye lens model described by CAHVORE was not directly corrected for distortions,
but rather warped to a map.
D.2.1
LVS
This part is based on [1].
Hardware side
On the hardware side, then there are two packages added to Mars 2020 design, the LCAM
and Vision Compute Element. The LCAM has already been described section 2.1. This
camera was chosen due to requirements that other cameras with heritage, weren’t able
to meet such as:A benefit for this camera is that the purpose is only to operate during the
entry, descent and landing phase, meaning that it doesn’t have to operate in the Martian
environment for very long, which in turns means that the temperature and dust protection
and testing isn’t needed like it’s the case for the surface cameras, and therefore also
reduce the mass slightly.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
127
==PAGE:144==
D.2. What has been accomplished ­ Focus on Mars 2020 LVS
Figure D.1: Overview of LVS taken from [1], original caption: Fig. 1 LVS concept of
operations during EDL.
The Vision compute element on the other hand is the computer that do the image process­
ing and position estimations. This is done by using IMU data from the descent IMUs which
is providing data to the rover computer too. The Vision Compute Element (VCE) uses a
BAE RAD750 processor and a Xilinx Virtex 5 FPGA for image processing. Having a ded­
icated computing unit for the sole purpose of pose estimation will make the design more
robust, as computer vision tends to use more resources than normal tasks like house­
keeping data and measuring different values. Another benefit of the VCE, is that it was
reprogrammable, such that it could be used for stereo vision afterwards for surface nav­
igation. on the surface. And having a separate module certainly also makes the testing
and development of the system much easier.
Dedicated IMUs has not been added to the hardware side, as stated in the paper, where
the mass reduction and high reliability from using the onboard Descent IMUs (DIMUs) was
selected to be the best way. However, this can lead to some sources of errors especially
since there is no rigid connection between the LCAM and those DIMUs, especially not
when deployment starts.
Software side
On the software side, then there are two main operational modes as stated. Both of these
modes require a reference map. The first mode is used to coarsely reduce the horizontal
error from 3.2 km down to 200 m from three images when operating normally. This may
be extended if there are problems when finding good matches. This phase uses a coarse
map, which has the spatial resolution of 12 m pixel. After this phase, then it continues to
operate in the fine pose estimation. This mode is characterized by having a higher spatial
resolution of 6 m/pixel, which goal is to reduce the final pose estimation error down to
under 40 m. The attitude estimation is expected to be very good thanks to the DIMUs. The
two main differences between coarse and fine mode, is that the coarse mode relies on a
coarse map (and larger patches) and uses frequency­based image correlation, where the
fine mode uses the full resolution map (and smaller patches) combined with spatial image
128
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:145==
Appendix D. Additional things
Figure D.2: The LVS hardware and used components taken from [1], original caption: Fig.
3 LVS flight hardware.
correlation. The features/patches are found from using the Harris interest operator/Harris
corner detection, which was chosen due to the heritage from previous the generation of
TRN called DIMES and simplicity of implementation, compared to SIFT and SURF.
Coarse mode
Horizontal error reduction from 3.2 km down to 200 m
As it can be seen from the top part of Figure D.3, then it can be seen that the coarse
mode consist of three modules; sequencer, coarse mode processing and finally the EKF.
The task of the sequencer is to sequence the data such that they are in order and timed
correctly, and that f.ex. the spacecraft state is only provided once in the beginning of the
process. This is needed for the batch estimator (filter module), since the IMU data rate is
200 Hz which is much faster than the images that is up to 1 Hz. The task of the module
with the batch estimator is to provide a horizontal position estimate. This is done by prop­
agating the IMU which measures the linear acceleration and angular rates and therefore
needs to be integrated in order to achieve a horizontal position estimate. This propagation
typical results in errors may accumulate, result in the initial horizontal estimation error of
3.2 km, which is why the batch estimator is used in order to use the estimate from the
three coarse images. For the most interesting module/part, then the estimation process
in the top left corner of Figure D.3 makes all the TRN vision part. In here it can be seen
that the input is the LVS state (pose estimate), camera model, sun vector and map. Then
it’s selects features (by using Harris corner detection) from 64x64 windows, and warping
the image such that the effects of distortion for the points gets corrected. The features
then gets downselected based on the distance to other points and the score from Harris.
At the same time, then the reference map is cropped. The 5 patches/features are then
correlated with the cropped reference map (and downscaled). Then outliers are rejected
when 3 images have been processed and 15 points has been found. This step involves
a comparison between where each point is transformed to the map as a search area and
where the correlation peak is. This will not be explained further, as it’s more complex
than what has been developed during this thesis, however, the different equations and
algorithm is described in ..... After outlier detection and rejection, then up to 15 landmark
matches are used for the estimation of horizontal position in the batch estimator. This
leads to the fine mode.
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
129
==PAGE:146==
D.2. What has been accomplished ­ Focus on Mars 2020 LVS
Figure D.3: Functional diagram of LVS taken from [1], original caption: Fig. 6 The MRL
algorithms perform coarse localization to take out a large position error and a fine local­
ization to obtain the required position accuracy.
Fine mode
Horizontal error reduction from 200 m down to 40 m or less
The fine mode or processing can be seen in the bottom part of Figure D.3. The main
components are looking very similiar, with the same kind of modules. The sequencer
now provides the horizontal position update from the coarse mode. The filter module
containing the EKF now outputs a pose estimate along with a velocity estimate at 1 Hz.
The EKF works by defining a model, where the uncertainties of the sensor/estimates
inputs are used to provide estimates that has lower uncertainties. The pose estimation
module on the left, is more simple than for the coarse mode, as the feature selection is
still happening but smaller templates/features are selected, and correlated in the spatial
domain. The reference map is once again cropped, but not rescaled. Then it’s followed
by an outlier detection and rejection, which is done by using the 3D points and using
the RANSAC procedure by testing many different combinations of three points and using
the highly accurate DIMUs to make a special version of a triangulation algorithm which
doesn’t require SVD. The outlier detection and rejection is only possible due to the 150
matches/features found in this mode, as it would be very efficient for coarse mode with
such a low amount of matches/features. The matches/feature is then used in the EKF to
130
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:147==
Appendix D. Additional things
estimate the pose and velocity. Like it was the case of the coarse mode, then this will not
explain in further details, is far more complex compared to what has been developed and
worked with during the thesis, however, the different equations and algorithm is described
in the paper.
Requirements and design limits
Reduce an initial horizontal error of 3.2 km at the height of 4.2 km, down to a horizontal
error of 40 m or less in 10 s
Timing
Due to LVS having a requirement to reach a certain estimation accuracy within a time
constraint, then timing was essential to manage this. This resulted in the timing table in
Figure D.4, where the tasks are shown, and every task is done sequential and therefore
no parallel tasks. The exact timing is not shown in this diagram, however, the timing
is found by predicting the worst case, like if something is delayed. Since the tasks are
sequential and the timing is based on worst case, then as it’s stated in ..., it doesn’t matter
if a task takes 50 % or 80 % of the allocated time, as the next task will take place only
at the predefined time. In the figure it can be seen that the systems starts by cropping
the map, and then starts with the first image exposure, followed by transfer to the FPGA
and image processing such that features are found and matched. This is image exposure
and processing is done two more times, which is then followed by outlier detection and
rejection, batch estimation with filter of the data (using the IMU). After the coarse phase is
ended, then the new fine phase is started. During the fine phase, then the same procedure
is done, with the difference that outlier detection and rejection is done for every image,
and that the filter and spacecraft state is updated for every image. This is then done until
reaching some constraints like deployment height. For this thesis work, then this hasn’t
been considered as the project was decided to not have a hard limit on time constrain.
Verification and validation
As it’s stated in the report from Johnson et al[1], then the verification and validation ap­
proach for the LVS subsystem of Mars 2020, was Trifecta, which is based on the three
categories; simulation, testbed and field test. This approach can be done such that sim­
ulation models are validated from field test, where those can be used to look at difficult
cases for the model which may require special tests in the lab or in the field. This is ac­
companied by testing with the approach of Test As You Fly (TAYF), stress testing and
worry beads. TAYF means that test resembles the actual intended environment that the
system is to be working in, meaning that ideally no corrections/exceptions shall be made
for how it works. This can be difficult to do when the system is to be used in the Martian
atmosphere, however, it should be tested as close to this as possible. For the stress
testing, then it can be anything from timing and load of the computing unit, to external
interfaces and environment. Worry beads are, according to the paper, referring to spe­
cific phenomena like dust in the Martian atmosphere. LVS used many different tests, from
simulations, using real Mars data, helicopter tests, sounding rocket test data. The result
from a helicopter test in 2019 can be seen in Figure D.5a, where the LVS testing model
consisted of an engineering model which functioned as the one that was going to be in the
rover. This resulted in horizontal position errors being lower than the required 40 m, which
means that the system worked as intended. The importance of this test was that the he­
licopter test was at different terrains and lighting conditions, meaning that the simulation
done was validated, as the Trifecta was about (using one to confirm the other). However,
the simulation part is as important, as it’s impossible to test for any possible condition that
may happen under the descent phase. This is where several simulations can be done
with different conditions, with both lighting, pose and artefacts like the heat shield that
covers a part of the image. The result from a series of Monte Carlo simulations can be

very good results which confirms that the system should work in the indented conditions,

Results
Mars 2020 demonstrated that high precision landing can be done on Mars, which has
no GNSS. This is very evident when looking at the results, but firstly the result from the
features/patches for both the coarse and fine operation mode is shown in Figure D.6.
Here it can be seen that 5 patches (green rectangles) are found in the coarse mode for
the first image. It can also be seen that red lines indicates the area, which marks the area
that the image (top right image) covers, which is actual the warping of the image onto the
map which arises from the highly distorted image. The blue rectangle marks the cropped
region of the map. During the fine mode, then even more matches are found, where the
outliers are now marked as the squares which isn’t blue or green. This is similar to the
coarse case, with the clear difference that the area enclosed by the red lines is smaller
than the coarse mode, which should be the case when Mars 2020 gets near the surface.

Figure D.7a, where the starting of the estimated pose starts a good amount of time be­
fore the Map Relative Localization (MRL) starts (around 35 s[1, table 3]. After the start
of MRL at 4.2 km, then there is a period where the coarse estimation is working in the
background as the timing in Figure D.4 showed, before a coarse estimate provides a sig­
nificant change along the x­axis, and smaller in the y­axis. This is showed as the jump
between the orange (LVS estimates) and purple line (assumed estimates without using
LVS). The velocity doesn’t change when using the LVS, which may be due to the IMU
being quite good and with the Doppler radar that provide a velocity in different directions.
This is maybe also the case of the attitude, that the orientation in space is well known

before (which can be done in many different ways). The cruise stage did have a star
scanner and sun sensor (not enough information to clearly describe these), which may
should provide a good estimate before separation of this stage. Another key difference
is that from the gyro sensor (measures angular rate) and accelerometer (measures ac­
celeration), then only a single integration is needed to yield the corresponding angle and
velocity, where the position requires a double integration of the acceleration which then
is even more sensitive to noise in the acceleration. It can be seen from the figures that
during the last position estimate that has been used for the STS. After a couple of sec­

500 m, where it will then enter a mode where only images are captured for the purpose
of using them for future missions. Figure D.7b on the other hand shows the differences

by using the data from the DIMU and optical observations) and the state estimates from
the LVS/onboard states. When looking at the positions, then the jump after the coarse es­

also showed a small impact. The jump in position is estimated to be 137 m according to
the paper, which is significant larger than the required 40 m or less, however this could
have been up to 3.2 km. It can however be seen that it’s quickly reduced, which according
to the paper is around 6 s. The velocity is also very stable between ±0.25s, and as the
article mention, then the pixel resolution of the map is 6 m/pixel, which therefore is a great
accomplishment. The attitude offset is also small, which the article confirms, since the er­
ror of 0.05° in the x and z component corresponds to less than 0.5 pixel. For comparison,
then 0.05° corresponds to a 20kr coin being placed around 31 m away. The article com­
ments to the converging y component(−0.1°) as being a misalignment between the DIMU
and LCAM. Furthermore, the changes in the other components at around 26 s, may be
due to a sudden change in the alignment of the DIMU and LCAM which happened during
the backshell separation and thruster firing initial. This effect was however temporarily,
as the article also notes, which is still very low.
D.2.2
Reference map
The LVS system uses an onboard reference map consisting of a DEM and SRM as men­
tioned in section 2.1. The SRM is basically just a photometrical calibrated orthophoto tied
to a reference frame where the pixels indicate the surface reflectance. This part will be
based on the paper from Cheng et al. which explains the procedure for making the maps
for the Mars 2020 mission.[37]. Due to the surface having a curvature, then it should be
noted that projection is onto a tangentplane(epirectangular projection) as mentioned in
section 3.7, which makes it possible to have square pixels in a grid. The LVS system
have certain requirements for the reference map, which will be shown in the following
part. All of these parts are based on [37].
Requirements
For the requirements for making the reference maps, then the full list is shown in ap­
pendix Table B.1, however the most important requirements involve the size, resolution
etc. Therefore, the following are the key highlights:
• Pixels scale of the SRM should be 6 m/pixel, which should be the scale at operation
altitude of 3 km above the terrain, and the DEM resolution should be 6 m/pixel.
• The maps should cover an area which has the size of 30 km × 30 km, to cover the
entire trajectory and ensure coverage.
• The map projection used is the orthographic projection
134

from [1], original caption: Fig.

Figure D.7: The performance results from LVS on Mars 2020 during EDL
• The elevation from the DEM is defined as the distance perpendicular from the Mar­
tian surface to the local tangent plane
• The absolute horizontal error of the map should be less than 300 m in the tangent
plane compared to the MCMF inertial coordinate frame.
Data sources
The requirements are then used to choose a dataset which should be used to form the
maps. Here it was chosen to use the MRO CTX Imager as the primary data source, which
according to ..., matches the pixel scale and swath. Furthermore, different sources like
MOLA were also used to validate the map. The MRO CTX Imager consist of 5064 pixels
CCD sensor, which works like a ’pushbroom’ camera, or in other words scans/image the
surface as it flies above. One of the biggest challenges according to the paper is that there
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
135
==PAGE:152==
D.2. What has been accomplished ­ Focus on Mars 2020 LVS
isn’t a ground truth model of Mars which very good resolution like on Earth. This means
that it’s not possible to get an exact model with great resolution, like on Earth. This will also
be the case for other planetary bodies. In order to solve this, then the different sources are
used, where f.ex. MOLA is used to provide a highly accurate vertical measurement( 1 m)
at the resolution of 463 m/pixel. This exact procedure using the extra data sources is
written in the report as:
”Specifically, we use MOLA to evaluate absolute elevation error, HRSC to test
the absolute map position, and HiRISE to assess local distortion and relative
elevation error.”
This means that the different data sources are used to reduce the errors such as the
absolute and relative elevation errors, local distortions and the position on the map. It’s not
well described with the exact procedure, however, two versions of the LVS were created
by two different teams; JPL and USGS Astrogeology Science Center team, which then
were compared to reveal errors in either the procedure, the input data or other types of
errors.
General method
• Identify all possible error sources related to the final map product.
• Remove or mitigate errors.
• Choose the best input data.
• Create two independent maps of the same site.
• Compare the final map product with other maps.
The first step involves the identification of the sources of error. This should be used
to reduce the effect of these errors in the next step. In table 2 of the report, it can be
seen the attitude errors identified are relative small(35 µrad), which mainly arises from
the uncertainty for the attitude of the spacecraft (data source). The error in the position, is
found from two sources; the orbital position uncertainty(10 m), and jitter effect from using
a pushbroom camera(5 m). Furthermore, it was found that the camera model of MRO
CTX can have up to 10 pixel displacement error, and clock error of 20 ms.
The next step is to remove or at least mitigate those errors. Some errors are easier to
manage than others. The report states that errors related to the time, camera models
etc. are easier to remove than f.ex. jitter from using the pushbroom. During this step,
then different methods were used to remove those, while the jitter was more difficult.
The jitter effect is caused by small oscillations and vibrations of the spacecraft of higher
frequencies. More specifically, then sampling frequency of the two star trackers are 10 Hz,
laser based IMU of 200 Hz, and due to the pushbroom camera, then the line scanning
frequency is 500 Hz where the attitude is interpolated, which is therefore identified as
the biggest source of error to the jittering. The way they mitigate this error source, is to
use several images, as the noise is approximately zero mean gaussian, with therefore
can be averaged to reduce the jittering. In addition to this, they use similar to image
median filtering to first remove bad matches or pointing and then average after that median
filtering. When looking at their results, then the effect is heavily reduced, as the gaps gets
to fit within the requirements.
At the third step, then the input data is chosen such that quality is well within the require­
ments. This involves the specific condition of the time the data was measured/exposed,
and image coverage. At the fourth step, then the two versions of the map are created
independently. This will ensure that the final map will have as small errors as possible, as
136
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:153==
Appendix D. Additional things
the comparison of the two produced maps will reveal errors due to the methods or input
data. The final step is to compare the final map with other maps. As mentioned, then
different sources are used to validate certain aspects. No other map exists from a single
data source which fits all the requirements, which means that only certain aspects can
be validated by each data source. As it was written in the report, then MOLA, HRSC and
HiRISE has been used to evaluate and validate this map.
As this thesis works with a more simple case and a different kind of data input, then the
specific method is not explained in details, as the information appears to be very sparse.
However, the method does involve similar steps such as combining different DEM with
overlaps, where it’s based on a weighted average with a similar weighting model of one
over the distance to the border. The other steps include ”triangulation, bad data removal,
hole filling, DEM construction from point clouds, and image orthorectification”, which all
are very similar to the developed method.
The resulted map was the one from USGS, as the map was deemed the best and due to
the long experience of making maps for planetary landers. It was found that the absolute
horizontal map error was offsetted from the HRSC ND5 reference orthophoto, meaning
that the orthophoto and DEM was shifted 17.9614 m west and 20.2454 m north to align,
which resulted in an absolute horizontal map error close to zero, and at the same time
improve the similarity of the LVS DEM and the HRSC DEM. The orientation error was
also validated to be within 1 mrad, as the orientation error between the two versions of
the final map was less than 0.2 mrad, and between the version from USGS and the HRSC
map is 0.8 mrad. Another test was to use the USGS map to test for the orientation with
the MRO spacecraft orientation, where it was found once again to well within the 1 mrad
requirement. For the elevation map(DEM), then it was validated and correct with the data
from HRSC and MOLA. A systematic error of 190 m was found which was corrected for,
but the source was not found. After this correction, then it was found that the difference
between the elevation was well within the requirements. Of other outputs from this map
generation work, then the camera model of MRO CTX was improved to reduce the errors,
and making a new model to correct for the pushbroom jittering.
D.2.3
Hazards
In order for Mars 2020 to land safely, then hazards should be avoided. This was done by
STS, which used an on­board map of hazards by defining hazardous score depending on
the slope and rock sizes of the terrain. The paper from Brugarolas et al. [10], will be used
here to describe the systems, with addition of [41].
Hazards
The hazard map is made from the three parts; slopes, rocks and inescapable obstacles/­
hazards. The slopes can be fund from DEM, where it should be remembered that it’s
perpendicular to the tangent plane and therefore the curvature should be remembered.
The rocks are a whole other category, as the larger rocks can be seen from space, but
smaller rocks(0.5 −1.5m) can not easily be seen with the current orbiters to give a good
estimate on their position, which is then found from rock distribution models. Larger rocks
and inescapable obstacles can be mapped from space.
Hazards score
The hazardous score is defined in certain ways: For slopes higher than 35°, inescapable
hazards and large rocks with a diameter larger than 1.5 m is set to 100 % of mission failure.
For slopes between 25° and 35°, then the mission failure rate is 15 %. Lastly, for the range
of 0 % to 20 % is assigned for medium­sized rocks(0.5 −1.5m) based on rock distribution
models. The hazard scores are found from the above, where it’s then padded such that 4
different colour classes can be used, at the intervals; < 0.75 %, 0.75 %−1.25 %, 1.25 %−
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
137
==PAGE:154==
D.2. What has been accomplished ­ Focus on Mars 2020 LVS
4 %, and finally > 4 %. These four intervals are coloured from green, yellow, orange and
red. Furthermore, a slope map is also used for getting the slope such that if the risk at
two points is the same, then the one with a flatter terrain is chosen. The slope criteria
are added to the hazards score, such that a mask is made with benign slopes of up to
10°, and then the other mask with slopes higher than 10°. The hazards score is then
added to 8­bit map called Safe Targets Map (STM)(20 km × 20 km), where the hazards
are encoded with the intensity values from 0 to 255 where the levels are defined as the
list below, with the addition of masking between benign slopes and non­benign slopes.
It should be noted that the actual 8­bit STM is encoded such that the lowest risk to the
mission have the highest level number.
• Levels every 0.01 % from 0 % to 0.5 % padded landing risks
• Levels every 0.05 % from 0.5 % to 2 % padded landing risks
• Levels every 0.2 % from 2 % to 5 % padded landing risks
• Levels every 1 % from 5 % to 10 % padded landing risks
• Levels every 5 % from 10 % to 100 % padded landing risks
STS
To explain how the hazards are used in combination with TRN, then, the STS will de­
scribed further. STS is the function that chooses where to land, which have heritage from
previous missions. The STS will firstly estimate the zero divert point, which the landing
spacecraft is expected to land at if no divertion is to be done. This will typical be the case
for the backshell which is why the divertion manure is needed in order to reduce the re­
contact risk with the backshell. After the zero divert point has been estimated, then the
STS will start to search for safe places to land. This is done by looking in two wedges
centred around the zero­divert point. A study to examine the size of the wedge found that
the inner radius should be 300 m, outer radius of 635 m and angles of ±60°. This wedge
is then used to limit the search area further. Since the rover uses propulsive divertion and
there is limited amount of fuel, then some care must be taken to this. In addition, then the
backshell recontact risk should also be taken care of, as the backshell is most likely to be
around the zero divert point (lower distance is worse) and the risk increases likewise in
the travel direction (wedge angles close to 90°). The wedges are therefore only used to
limit the search area for safe landing spots, and take into account the amount of fuel avail­
able, and backshell recontact risk. The wedges are also noted as the prime and reserve
wedges. This means that the prime wedge is always preferred if the safe target point
in this wedge have the highest safety score. The reverse wedge is only used when it’s
safer than the prime wedge by a threshold. In addition, then the STS was developed such
that it will divert the rover 467.5 m in the prime wedge direction if problems arises, like if
LVS doesn’t work as intended or the STM gets corrupted. This is the same mode back
from previous missions. During the actual usage onboard Mars 2020, then STS worked
exactly as intended, as two points were found in the prime and reverse wedges from the
zero divert point, where the prime wedge point had a safety level of 82(1.9 % risk), and
the reverse wedge had a safety level of 104(1.35 % risk). This resulted that the point in
the reverse wedge was chosen, and the exact re­estimation of the zero divert point was
done and the divert correction was provided to the powered approach profiler onboard
which handled the final approach and manoeuvring. The re­estimations of the zero divert
point was actually in the beneficial direction, as it travel in direction of the target, which
ended up being slightly smaller divertion and thus save fuel. In the report, it was likewise
also stated that if the STS from Mars Science Laboratory (MSL) was used without the
TRN ability, then a divertion of 300 m in the direction of the prime wedge would have re­
138
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:155==
Appendix D. Additional things
sulted in a safety level of only 34(20 % risk), which would be far more risky, which exactly
showed how important TRN was to the mission success. It was further analysed later that
the area around in the landing spots was well described by the STM as no major hazards
were missing.

function [Z,R] = autoCrop(Z,R,position ,orientation ,size,ZDTM,RDTM,Flat,Radius)
2
% [Z,R] = autoCrop(Z,R,position ,orientation ,size,ZDTM,RDTM,Flat,Radius)
3
% Crops the map based on the position and orientation
4
%
5
%
6
% Inputs:
7
%
Z:
The reference map/image

Reference to the map
9

The estimated position

The estimated orientation
11
%

The final size in pixels

The 3D surface

Reference to the 3D surface

The flattening of Mars

The equatorial radius of Mars

%

%

19
%
Z:
Cropped reference map/image
20
%
R:
Reference to the cropped map/image

% Last modified 02-06-2022

32

Radius);

35

40

function [output] = CAHVORE_IMG(header,pixel_size ,image_width ,image_height)
2
% Usage: [output] = CAHVORE_IMG(header ,pixel_size ,image_width ,image_height)
3
% Extracts the CAHVORE camera model from the image header and converts into
4
% the intrinsics used by MATLAB , equations are shown in the report. Thsi
5
% doesn't include the ORE terms in the intrinsics.
6
%
7
% Inputs:
152
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
==PAGE:169==
Appendix E. Code
8
%
header:
The image header
9
%
pizel_size:
The pixel size
10
%
image_width:
The width of the images(horizontal)
11
%
image_height:
The height of the images(vertical)
12
%
13
% Outputs:
14
%
output:
Structure containg the intrinsics and the
15
%
individual CAHVORE terms
16
%
movMatdhedPoints:
The matched features in the test image
17
%
18
%
19
%
20
% Last modified 02-06-2022
21
% Author: Martin Rossau , s164058
22
%% Extracts the elements
23
C = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_1');
24
A = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_2');
25
H = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_3');
26
V = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_4');
27
O = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_5');
28
R = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_6');
29
E = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_7');
30
type = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_8');
31
parameter = extractfield(header.CAMERA_MODEL_DATAELEMENTS ,'MODEL_COMPONENT_9')
;
32
33
34
%% Calculation of the intrinsics
35
h_c = dot(A,H); %1c % Scalar(dot product)
36
v_c = dot(A,V); %1d % Scalar(dot product)
37
h_s = norm(cross(A,H)); %1e % Length of vector
38
v_s = norm(cross(A,V)); %1f % Length of vector
39
40
41
42
delta_x = pixel_size; % Assuming that the pixel size given by specs. is at
least right for one of the sides
43
F = h_s * delta_x;
44
45
delta_y = F / v_s;
46
47
48
49
x_0 = (h_c-(image_width)/2)*delta_x;
50
y_0 = ((image_height)/2-v_c)*delta_y;
51
52
53
54
k(1) = R(1);
55
k(2) = R(2)/F^2;
56
k(3) = R(3)/F^2;
57
58
59
60
imagewidth = [image_height ,image_width];
61
width = pixel_size*imagewidth;
62
f = [h_s,v_s];
63
64
principalPoint = [h_c,v_c];
65
%% Output formatting
66
output.C = C;
67
output.A = A;
Optical Surface Navigation on Planetary Bodies
153
==PAGE:170==
68
output.H = H;
69
output.V = V;
70
output.O = O;
71
output.R = R;
72
output.E = E;
73
output.f = f;
74
75
output.k = k;
76

output.intrinsics = cameraIntrinsics(f,principalPoint ,imagewidth);
